⚖️ Constitutional Law

Constitution of 2022Constitution of the Republic of Tunisia

Adopted: July 25, 2022 (referendum) | Published: August 18, 2022 | In force: August 2022
The current governing constitution, promulgated by Presidential Decree No. 2022-691 of August 17, 2022. Establishes a presidential system with a bicameral legislature (National Council of Regions and Districts + Assembly of People's Representatives). Supersedes the 1959 and 2014 constitutions as well as Decree-Law No. 2011-14 of March 23, 2011. Article 5 references Islam and "the aims of Pure Islam." Comprises 142 articles organized into 10 chapters.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1: Tunisia is a free, independent, sovereign state; Islam is its religion, Arabic its language, the republic its form of government
  • Art. 2: Tunisia is a civil state based on citizenship, the will of the people, and the supremacy of law
  • Art. 5: The state shall work to achieve the objectives of Pure Islam
  • Art. 29: Intellectual property is guaranteed
  • Art. 40: Freedom to form political parties, unions, and associations is guaranteed
  • Art. 42: Freedom of peaceful assembly and demonstration is guaranteed
  • Art. 86–90: President elected for 5-year term, limit of two terms
  • Repeals: 1959 Constitution, Decree-Law No. 2011-14, 2014 Constitution
Constitutional

Constitution of 2014 [Superseded]Constitution of the Republic of Tunisia

Adopted: January 26, 2014 | In force: February 2014 | Superseded: August 2022
Post-Arab Spring constitution adopted by the National Constituent Assembly with 200 votes for, 12 against, 4 abstentions. Comprised 149 articles across 10 chapters. Established a semi-presidential system. Article 74 restricted the presidency to Muslims. Now superseded by the 2022 Constitution.
Key provisions (historical)
  • Art. 1: Tunisia is a free, independent, sovereign state; Islam its religion, Arabic its language
  • Art. 2: Tunisia is a civil state based on citizenship, the will of the people, and the primacy of law
  • Art. 20: Equality before the law guaranteed without discrimination
  • Art. 46: Enshrined women's rights and gender parity in elected bodies
  • Art. 74: Presidential candidates must be Muslim, Tunisian nationals, born of Tunisian parents
  • Art. 88: Mechanism for presidential impeachment
  • Art. 115: Prime minister removal required majority of assembly members
ConstitutionalSuperseded

Constitution of 1959 [Superseded]Constitution of the Tunisian Republic

Promulgated: June 1, 1959 | In force: June 1, 1959 | Superseded: August 2022
Tunisia's first post-independence constitution. Modeled on the French system, vested strong executive power in a president. Arabic declared national language. Presidents elected for 5-year terms. Amended multiple times — notably in 2002 (removed presidential term limits, opened candidacy to persons over 75). In force for 63 years until replaced by the 2022 Constitution.
Key provisions (historical)
  • Art. 1: Tunisia is a free, independent, sovereign state; Islam its religion, Arabic its language, the republic its form
  • Art. 39: Presidential term set at 5 years
  • Art. 40: President elected by universal direct suffrage
  • 2002 amendment: removed two-term limit, raised age ceiling to 75
ConstitutionalSuperseded

Decree-Law No. 2011-14 of March 23, 2011Provisional Organization of Public Authorities

Enacted: March 23, 2011 | In force: March 2011 | Superseded: August 2022
Established the transitional constitutional framework following the 2011 revolution. Organized interim executive and legislative powers pending the drafting and adoption of a permanent constitution. Superseded by the 2022 Constitution.
ConstitutionalSuperseded

Presidential Decree No. 117 of September 22, 2021Exceptional Measures

Article 80 invoked: July 25, 2021 | Decree 2021-117: September 22, 2021 | ARP formally dissolved: March 30, 2022
On July 25, 2021 (Republic Day), President Kais Saied invoked Article 80 of the 2014 Constitution, dismissing PM Hichem Mechichi's government, freezing (not yet formally dissolving) the Assembly of the Representatives of the People (ARP), and lifting MPs' immunity — despite Article 80 itself requiring Parliament to remain in continuous session while invoked. Presidential Decree No. 2021-117 of September 22, 2021 then formalized and radically extended these powers: it suspended the 2014 Constitution except its preamble and Chapters 1–2 (general provisions; rights and freedoms), transferred all legislative power to the president (exercised via presidential decrees with the Council of Ministers), declared such decrees non-appealable (Art. 7), suspended the interim constitutional-review body (Art. 21), and tasked the president with drafting a new constitution for referendum (Art. 22). Run 44 correction: the ARP was not dissolved by this decree — it remained merely frozen until March 30, 2022, when Saied announced its formal dissolution hours after roughly 120 MPs defied the freeze in an online plenary session voting to repeal the exceptional measures. This 2021–2022 sequence is the direct legal predicate for the July 2022 constitutional referendum, the 2022 Constitution, and the judiciary/electoral decree-laws elsewhere in this reference.
ConstitutionalSuperseded

2022 Constitution, Articles 116–120Constitutional Court

Established by: 2022 Constitution | Replaces: defunct Constitutional Council
The 2022 Constitution establishes a Constitutional Court replacing the defunct Constitutional Council. Composed of 9 members: 3 appointed by president, 3 elected by parliament, 3 elected by Supreme Judicial Council. 9-year non-renewable terms, staggered. Reviews constitutionality of laws before promulgation and settles conflicts of competence. Under the 2022 Constitution, the Constitutional Court cannot rule on the validity of the president's exceptional measures (Art. 96). Status as of June 2026: still not established — neither this court nor its 2014-Constitution predecessor has ever become operational; legal scholars and the Venice Commission describe Tunisia as having effectively gone without a functioning constitutional review body since 2011. The ARP's General Legislation Committee resumed hearings in February 2026 on Organic Law Proposal No. 100/2025, a private member's bill to actually create the Court under Chapter VI of the 2022 Constitution; as of mid-2026 the proposal remained under committee review with no vote scheduled.
Constitutional

Local Council Elections of December 24, 2023Seating of the National Council of Regions and Districts (CNRD)

First-round vote: December 24, 2023 | Second round: January 2024 | Implements: 2022 Constitution bicameral structure
Tunisia's first elections held entirely under the 2022 Constitution's "bottom-up" local governance model. Voters elected 2,155 members across 279 local councils for 5-year terms via a majoritarian two-round system (turnout: 11.7% round one, 12.53% round two — record lows). Each local council designates a member by lot to a regional (governorate) council; the 24 regional councils each elect a representative to one of 5 district councils; the district and regional councils together indirectly elect the 77-member National Council of Regions and Districts (CNRD), the upper chamber of parliament envisioned by the 2022 Constitution. The CNRD held its inaugural session in 2023, completing the bicameral legislature alongside the Assembly of People's Representatives. Critics, including IFES and the Carnegie Endowment, describe the multi-tiered indirect system as insulating the upper chamber from direct accountability.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Décret n° 2025-165 of March 20, 2025Appointment of the Head of Government

Enacted: March 20, 2025 | Published: JORT No. 2025-033
Presidential decree appointing Sarra Zaafrani Zenzri as Cheffe du Gouvernement (Head of Government), replacing Kamel Madouri — the fourth such change since President Kais Saied assumed exceptional powers in July 2021. Zaafrani Zenzri, previously Minister of Equipment and Housing, is only the second woman to head a Tunisian government (after Najla Bouden, 2021–2023). No official reason was given by the Presidency; press coverage (L'Orient-Le Jour, Africanews) attributed the change to Saied's public dissatisfaction with government performance.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 2022-22 of March 21, 2022ISIE Reorganization

Enacted: March 21, 2022 | In force: March 2022
Amended Organic Law No. 2012-23 governing the Independent High Authority for Elections (ISIE). Reduced ISIE membership from 9 to 7 members; altered appointment procedure giving the president greater influence. Criticized by Venice Commission for undermining electoral independence. Issued under presidential decree-law authority (Decree 117). Update (Run 40): the seven members appointed under this decree-law by Presidential Decree No. 2022-459 of May 9, 2022 serve non-renewable 4-year terms; those terms expired May 9, 2026, and as of mid-2026 no successor members had been appointed and no formal renewal mechanism had been triggered, per CRLDHT legal commentary — a single-source finding, flagged here pending stronger corroboration in a future run, describing the electoral authority's legal status as unresolved.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 2022-55 of September 15, 2022Electoral Reform — Individual Candidacies System

Enacted: September 15, 2022 | In force: September 2022 | Applied in: December 2022 / January 2023 elections
Replaced the proportional closed-list system for legislative elections with a single-member constituency majority system under which candidates run as individuals, not on party lists. Abolished the gender parity requirement for electoral lists. Widely seen as structurally weakening political parties and reducing women's parliamentary representation. Applied in the December 17, 2022 (first round) and January 29, 2023 (runoffs) elections for the Assembly of People's Representatives, producing historically low turnout (~8.8%).
ConstitutionalAdministrative

📜 Civil Law & Law of Obligations

Code of Obligations and Contracts (COC) — 1906Majallat Al-Iltizamat wa-l-Uqud

Promulgated: December 15, 1906 (under French Protectorate) | In force: January 1, 1907
The foundational civil code governing contracts, torts, and obligations. Derived from the French civil law tradition, adapted for Tunisian context. Covers general theory of obligations, specific contracts (sale, lease, agency, loan, deposit, surety), and civil liability. Has been substantially amended multiple times since independence. Remains in force as the core private law instrument.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–106: General theory of obligations (formation, validity, effects, extinction)
  • Art. 13: Requirements for valid contract — capacity, consent, object, cause
  • Art. 62–80: Nullity of contracts (absolute vs. relative)
  • Art. 82–96: Effects of obligations; performance and non-performance
  • Art. 100: Damages for non-performance; principle of full reparation
  • Art. 222–262: Contract of sale (conditions, effects, warranty)
  • Art. 728–733: Civil liability for delict and quasi-delict
  • Art. 786–828: Mandate and agency
CivilAmended

Code of Civil and Commercial Procedure — 1959Majallat Al-Murafa'at Al-Madaniyya wa-l-Tijariyya

Enacted: 1959 | Multiple amendments
Governs civil and commercial litigation procedures including jurisdiction, service of process, evidence, trials, judgments, and appeals. Regulates enforcement of judgments and provisional measures. Organizes appeals to Courts of First Instance, Courts of Appeal, and the Court of Cassation.
Key provisions
  • Rules on jurisdiction: territorial and subject-matter
  • Provisional and conservatory measures
  • Rules of evidence and burden of proof
  • Default judgments and opposition procedures
  • Appeals and cassation procedures
  • Enforcement of domestic and foreign judgments
CivilCommercialAmended

Law of August 1, 1957Civil Status (Registry of Births, Deaths, Marriages)

Enacted: August 1, 1957 | In force: 1957 | Amended: multiple times
Establishes the civil registration system for Tunisian nationals including registration of births, deaths, marriages, divorces, and changes of nationality. Mandates registration within specified time limits. Organizes the function of civil status officers (officiers d'état civil).
CivilAdministrative

Nationality Code — Law No. 63-6 of February 28, 1963

Enacted: February 28, 1963 | In force: 1963 | Amended: 1975, 2002, 2010
Defines Tunisian nationality by birth, descent, naturalization, and reacquisition. Originally limited transmission of nationality through the paternal line; amended in 2010 to allow Tunisian mothers married to foreign nationals to transmit nationality to their children. Governs loss, renunciation, and deprivation of nationality.
Key provisions
  • Art. 6: Nationality acquired by birth to a Tunisian father (jus sanguinis)
  • Art. 12: Nationality acquired through naturalization (5-year residence requirement)
  • 2010 amendment: children of Tunisian mother and foreign father may acquire nationality
  • Art. 30–35: Loss and deprivation of Tunisian nationality
CivilNationality

Law No. 98-97 of November 27, 1998Private International Law

Enacted: November 27, 1998 | In force: 1998 | Supplements: COC Arts. 52–67
Governs conflict-of-laws rules in Tunisia. The 1998 law comprehensively codified private international law rules on jurisdiction, applicable law, and recognition of foreign judgments. Applies French-tradition choice-of-law methodology. Islamic law applies to personal status of Muslim Tunisians.
Civil

Code of Civil Procedure Title XI (1959, as amended)Enforcement of Judgments

Enacted: 1959 | Amended: multiple times
Governs compulsory enforcement of court judgments and notarial instruments. Establishes the role of huissiers de justice (bailiffs) in enforcement. Provides for seizure of movable and immovable property, garnishment of wages, and forced sale. Amended to streamline enforcement timelines and protect minimum wage from seizure.
CivilAdministrative

👪 Personal Status Law

The Code of Personal Status (CPS) is Tunisia's most internationally distinctive statute — it abolishes polygamy and replaced unilateral repudiation (talaq) with court-supervised divorce. It has been continuously in force since 1957.

Code of Personal Status (CPS) — Beylical Decree of August 13, 1956Majallat Al-Ahwal Al-Shakhsiyya

Enacted: August 13, 1956 (beylical decree under Lamine Bey) | In force: January 1, 1957 | Amended: 1959, 1966, 1981, 1993, 2017
Tunisia's landmark personal status code, principally drafted by President Habib Bourguiba. Abolished polygamy (criminal offense under Art. 18), replaced unilateral male repudiation (talaq) with court-supervised divorce, set minimum marriage age. The most progressive family law in the Arab world. Covers marriage, divorce, filiation, adoption (recognition of kafala), inheritance, guardianship, and wills. Never suspended or repealed despite political pressures (2011–2013). The 2022 Constitution has not altered its operation.
Key provisions
  • Art. 3: Marriage requires consent of both spouses; minimum age 18 (with exceptions)
  • Art. 5: Marriage must be registered and conducted before a civil status officer
  • Art. 18: Polygamy prohibited and criminalized — up to 1 year imprisonment + fine for marrying while already married
  • Art. 29–37: Divorce only by court order; grounds include mutual consent, harm, abandonment
  • Art. 31: Either spouse may initiate divorce; court-ordered alimony mandatory
  • Art. 46–67: Filiation and parentage; paternity presumption
  • Art. 68–78: Legal guardianship (tutelle) of minors
  • Art. 85–199: Inheritance rules (miras) — blends civil and Islamic principles
  • Art. 154–165: Guardianship over persons under disability
  • 1993 amendment: mother gains equal custody rights; divorced mothers may become guardians
  • 2017 amendment: allowed Muslim women to marry non-Muslim men (circular, not codified)
Personal StatusCivilAmended

Child Protection Code — Law No. 95-92 of November 9, 1995Majallat Himayat Al-Tifl

Enacted: November 9, 1995 | In force: 1995 | Amended: 2002, 2008
Comprehensive statute protecting minors from abuse, neglect, exploitation, and trafficking. Establishes the Children's Judge (juge des enfants) with jurisdiction over child protection cases. Covers child welfare, delinquency, protective placement, and guardianship. Implements Tunisia's obligations under the UN Convention on the Rights of the Child (ratified 1992).
Key provisions
  • Creates specialized Children's Judges and children's courts
  • Art. 24–30: Protective measures for children in danger
  • Art. 70–80: Juvenile delinquency proceedings; preference for rehabilitation over punishment
  • Art. 101: Prohibition of child labor below age 16 in principle
  • Art. 105–110: International child abduction; return mechanisms
Personal StatusCivil

Law No. 58-27 of March 4, 1958Kafala (Legal Sponsorship of Abandoned Children)

Enacted: March 4, 1958 | In force: 1958 | Amended: 1994
Regulates the kafala system — the legal guardianship of abandoned children (Tunisia does not recognize formal adoption in the Islamic sense). Allows Tunisian families to take in abandoned children as legal dependents. Creates rights and obligations equivalent to adoption without severing the child's original lineage.
Personal Status

Law No. 2017-58 of August 11, 2017Elimination of Violence Against Women

Enacted: August 11, 2017 | In force: February 2018
Landmark statute criminalizing all forms of violence against women — physical, psychological, sexual, and economic. Abolished the provision allowing rapists to escape prosecution by marrying their victim. Created specialized police units and prosecution procedures for gender-based violence. Established protective orders and victim shelters. Considered one of the most progressive anti-GBV laws in the Arab world.
Key provisions
  • Removed Art. 227 bis of the Penal Code (marriage escaping rape prosecution)
  • Defines and criminalizes economic, psychological, and sexual violence
  • Establishes specialized prosecution units for violence against women
  • Provides emergency protective orders for victims
  • Mandatory reporting obligations for health professionals
  • National Observatory on Violence Against Women created
Personal StatusCriminal

Presidential Commission Report (2018)Inheritance Equality Debate

Report issued: August 2018 | Proposal not enacted
In August 2018, President Essebsi proposed legislation granting equal inheritance rights to men and women (departing from Quranic inheritance shares retained in CPS). The proposal, while debated in the national dialogue, was not enacted into law before his death in 2019. The CPS inheritance provisions (Arts. 85–199) remain in force, blending Islamic and civil principles.
Personal Status

⚔️ Criminal Law & Procedure

Penal Code — Law of July 9, 1913 (as amended)Majallat Al-Qanun Al-Jaza'i

Originally enacted: July 9, 1913 (French Protectorate) | Revised: 1964, 1968, 1987, 1993, 1999, 2004, 2010, 2017
Tunisia's criminal code. Enacted under the French Protectorate in 1913, substantially revised after independence. Classifies offenses into crimes (felonies), délits (misdemeanors), and contraventions (infractions). Organized in two books: general provisions and specific offenses. Notable provisions include criminalization of same-sex relations (Art. 230), blasphemy provisions, and offenses against state security. Amended significantly in 2017 following the Violence Against Women Law.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–59: General principles — criminal liability, complicity, recidivism, penalties
  • Art. 52: Capital punishment available for certain crimes (murder, rape causing death, terrorism)
  • Art. 72–85: Offenses against state security (treason, espionage)
  • Art. 86: Attacks against the head of state or government
  • Art. 121: Prohibited associations
  • Art. 214: Abortion prohibited except to save mother's life
  • Art. 218: Homicide and serious assault
  • Art. 226: Offenses against public morals
  • Art. 226 bis: Defamation of public officials via new communication technologies
  • Art. 227: Rape — minimum 6 years imprisonment
  • Art. 230: Consensual same-sex relations criminalized — up to 3 years imprisonment
  • Art. 231–245: Theft, fraud, embezzlement
  • Art. 305: Receiving stolen goods
CriminalAmended

Code of Criminal Procedure — Law No. 68-23 of July 24, 1968

Enacted: July 24, 1968 | Published: JORT No. 32 (August 2–6, 1968) | Amended: Law No. 87-70 (Nov. 26, 1987), Law No. 93-114 (Nov. 22, 1993)
Governs all stages of criminal proceedings in Tunisia. Organized in five books covering: public action and preliminary investigation, trial court jurisdictions, extraordinary remedies (appeals, cassation), special procedures, and execution of sentences. Article 1 establishes that any infraction gives rise to public action aimed at applying penalties.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1: Any infraction triggers public action ("Toute infraction donne ouverture à une action publique")
  • Art. 13–14 (as amended by Law No. 87-70): Limits on garde à vue detention
  • Art. 47: Preliminary judicial investigation mandatory for crimes (felonies)
  • Art. 57: Rights of suspects during investigation
  • Art. 85–87 (as amended by Law No. 93-114): Preventive detention — initial 6-month maximum, extendable
  • Art. 106–107: Extended detention provisions
  • Book II: Jurisdiction of cantonal courts, courts of first instance, courts of appeal
  • Book III: Opposition, appeal, and cassation remedies
  • Book IV: Military courts, terrorism courts, special jurisdictions
  • Book V: Execution of criminal sentences
CriminalAmended

Organic Law No. 2015-26 of August 7, 2015Countering Terrorism and Suppression of Money Laundering (as amended)

Enacted: August 7, 2015 | In force: August 2015 | Amended: Organic Law No. 2019-9 of January 23, 2019
Date/title correction: Previously listed as "Law No. 2015-26 of July 7, 2015" — primary sources (legislation-securite.tn, UNODC, OHCHR, ecolex) confirm the enactment date is August 7, 2015, and the official title covers both terrorism and money laundering in a single statute (this is also the same underlying law separately, and incorrectly, cited under a fictitious "Law No. 2019-23" on the former Anti-Money Laundering entry below — now corrected/merged). Enacted following the Bardo Museum and Sousse beach attacks (2015), replacing the 2003 anti-terrorism law. Defines terrorism broadly, criminalizes financing, propaganda, recruitment, and travel for terrorist purposes; establishes specialized anti-terrorism courts and the Financial Intelligence Unit (CTAF — Commission Tunisienne des Analyses Financières, created by Art. 118). Organic Law No. 2019-9 (Jan. 23, 2019) strengthened beneficial-ownership/PEP definitions, judicial interception rules, targeted financial sanctions, and NPO foreign-funding oversight — reforms credited with Tunisia's October 2019 removal from the FATF grey list. Criticized by human rights groups for overly broad definitions and extended pre-charge detention.
Key provisions
  • Broad definition of "terrorist act" including threats to public order
  • Criminalization of glorification of terrorism and incitement
  • Criminalizes financing of terrorism and related financial flows; establishes AML/CFT obligations on banks, DNFBPs, notaries, and exchange bureaus
  • Prohibition on travel to conflict zones for terrorist purposes
  • Specialized Terrorism Chamber at the Court of First Instance of Tunis
  • Extended detention periods for terrorism suspects
  • Asset freezing without prior judicial authorization
CriminalSecurityFinancialAmended

Organic Law No. 2017-10 of March 7, 2017Declaration of Assets and Conflict of Interest

Enacted: March 7, 2017 | In force: 2017
Requires elected officials, senior public servants, and government appointees to declare assets, income, and interests. Creates the National Anti-Corruption Authority (INLUCC — Instance Nationale de Lutte contre la Corruption). Whistleblower protections included. Status as of June 2026: INLUCC's funding and operations were curtailed following the July 2021 presidential power consolidation, and unlike under the 2014 Constitution, the 2022 Constitution contains no provision establishing a constitutionally protected anti-corruption body — INLUCC has continued under a presidentially appointed interim head (since October 2022) with no permanent statutory replacement enacted, leaving Tunisia without an independent constitutionally entrenched anti-corruption authority.
CriminalAdministrative

Anti-Money Laundering and Financing of TerrorismSee Organic Law No. 2015-26 (as amended)

Citation corrected — see below
Correction: This entry previously cited a standalone "Law No. 2019-23 of March 6, 2019" as Tunisia's AML/CFT statute — no such law could be verified against any primary source (JORT, legislation-securite.tn, FATF documentation), and it appears to have been a citation error, possibly conflating the amending law's year (2019) with the base law's number (26). Tunisia's actual, sole AML/CFT legal instrument is Organic Law No. 2015-26 of August 7, 2015 ("relative à la lutte contre le terrorisme et à la répression du blanchiment d'argent"), as amended by Organic Law No. 2019-9 of January 23, 2019 — FATF itself identifies it as "Organic Law Number 26 for the year 2015 relating to Countering Terrorism and Stopping Money Laundering." See the full entry under Criminal Law above for provisions, KYC/beneficial-ownership obligations, and the Financial Intelligence Unit (CTAF). This card is retained as a cross-reference so a reader searching "money laundering" or "AML" still finds the correct law.
CriminalFinancialAmended

Law No. 92-52 of May 18, 1992Narcotics and Psychotropic Substances

Enacted: May 18, 1992 | In force: 1992 | Amended: 1999, 2017
Governs narcotics and psychotropic substances including cultivation, production, trafficking, possession, and use. Imposes severe penalties — mandatory minimum 1 year for consumption (Art. 4), up to life imprisonment for trafficking. Article 4 on personal drug use attracted international criticism; the 2017 amendment introduced some discretion for judges in consumption cases. Pending reform (Run 45): a 17-article amendment bill, filed by roughly 15 deputies and transmitted to committee in April 2025, would combine expanded investigative tools against trafficking networks with a shift toward a therapeutic/medical approach to simple consumption; the Tunisian Society for Addiction Studies (STADD) presented its position at a May 22, 2026 committee hearing chaired by Faouzi Daas. Still in committee as of mid-2026; not yet enacted.
Key provisions
  • Art. 4: Personal consumption of narcotics — 1 to 5 years imprisonment + fine (mandatory)
  • Art. 5: Trafficking — 6 years to life imprisonment
  • Art. 12: Confiscation of proceeds and instruments of offenses
  • 2017 amendment: judges given discretion on sentencing for first-time consumption offenses
Criminal

Decree No. 2022-54 of September 13, 2022Combating Information Technology Offenses

Enacted: September 13, 2022 | In force: September 2022
Criminalizes "false information" spread online that "harms third parties, public order, or national defense." Criticized by press freedom organizations for its vague language and potential to criminalize journalism and free speech. Passed by presidential decree without parliamentary scrutiny.
CriminalDigitalPress

Law No. 2017-62 of July 24, 2017Administrative Reconciliation Law

Enacted: July 24, 2017 | In force: 2017
Controversial statute granting amnesty and legal impunity to civil servants who participated in corruption under the Ben Ali regime (1987–2011) in exchange for voluntary financial restitution to the state. Allowed implicated officials to resume positions in the public administration. Strongly opposed by civil society and transitional justice advocates as undermining accountability.
CriminalAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 2022-13 of March 20, 2022Criminal Reconciliation (Saied-Era)

Enacted: March 20, 2022 | Published: JORT No. 30, March 21, 2022 | Amended: Law No. 2024-3 of January 18, 2024
Distinct from the 2017 Administrative Reconciliation Law above: creates the National Commission for Criminal Reconciliation (CNRP), allowing businesspeople and officials accused of corruption, embezzlement, money laundering, or tax/customs/exchange-control violations to end prosecution by repaying misappropriated funds (plus 10%/year interest) or financing local/regional development projects. Targeted an initial list of roughly 460 businesspeople accused of misappropriating TND 10–13.5 billion. Amended by Law No. 2024-3 (passed by the ARP, 126 votes for), attaching the CNRP to the Presidency, adding two vice-presidents, and setting a 4-month maximum review period for applications. Update (Run 46): President Saied relaunched the process by swearing in a fully reconstituted CNRP before him at Carthage Palace on June 27, 2026, chaired by magistrate Ali Abbès; the appointment was formalized through Presidential Decree No. 2026-109 of June 8, 2026 (naming the chair) and Decree No. 131/2026, published in JORT on June 26, 2026, completing the commission's membership. At the swearing-in, Saied reaffirmed his framing of criminal reconciliation as a mechanism for recovering misappropriated public funds rather than a general amnesty.
CriminalCommercial

Decree-Law No. 2022-14 of March 20, 2022Anti-Speculation Decree-Law

Enacted: March 20, 2022 | Published: JORT No. 30, March 21, 2022
Criminalizes hoarding or concealing goods to create artificial scarcity or manipulate prices, and criminalizes spreading "false news" that causes consumers to avoid buying goods or disrupts supply chains. Standard penalty up to 10 years' imprisonment plus a TND 100,000 fine; aggravated penalties of 10–30 years for hoarding subsidized staples (e.g., flour). Amnesty International warned in March 2022 that the broad "false news" provision risks chilling public debate on food shortages.
CriminalCommercial

Law No. 2025-14 of July 28, 2025Penal Code Amendment — Abuse of Function

Enacted: July 28, 2025 | Published: JORT No. 96, July 29, 2025
Amends Articles 96 and 98 of the Penal Code governing "abuse of function" by public officials. Reduces the maximum prison sentence under Article 96 from 10 years to 6 years and converts the offense from strict liability to an intentional one, requiring proof the official deliberately sought an undue advantage to the detriment of the administration. Originated from 2023 parliamentary proposals; framed as reducing the "chilling effect" of Article 96 on civil servants' investment and administrative decisions.
CriminalAmended

Draft Amendment to Penal Code Arts. 261, 262 & 264 (pending)Harsher Theft & Aggravated-Robbery Penalties

Under General Legislation Committee review, hearings confirmed April 7 and May 16, 2026 | Status: not yet enacted as of July 2, 2026
A pending bill that would repeal and replace Penal Code Articles 261, 262, and 264 (theft, aggravated theft, and embezzlement), sharply raising penalties: new Art. 261 would impose 15–20 years' imprisonment for aggravated theft committed under the circumstances numbered 1, 2, or 5 of Article 260 (e.g., theft with breaking-and-entering, by an armed person, or at night by two or more persons); new Art. 262 would impose 12–15 years for the circumstances numbered 3 or 4 of Article 260; new Art. 264 would set 5 years' imprisonment plus a 2,000 TND fine for lesser theft and embezzlement offenses. The draft excludes the mitigating-circumstance provisions currently available under the existing articles, a feature the Syndicat des Magistrats Tunisiens has publicly criticized as disproportionate and likely to remove judicial discretion in minor cases. Distinct from Law No. 2025-14 (July 28, 2025, above), which amended unrelated Penal Code Articles 96 and 98 on public officials' abuse of function. Still in committee hearings as of mid-May 2026; not yet enacted.
CriminalPending

Law No. 2011-29 of April 18, 2011Confiscation Law

Enacted: April 18, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Amended by Law 2011-68
Enacted post-revolution to confiscate assets of Ben Ali, his family, and associates. Established a National Commission for the Investigation of Corruption and Embezzlement (CNIICF). Assets confiscated included real estate, bank accounts, vehicles, and corporate shares. Managed by the Tunisian Privatisation Authority.
CriminalAdministrative

Law No. 2016-61 of August 3, 2016Anti-Human Trafficking Law

Enacted: August 3, 2016 | In force: 2016
Comprehensive statute criminalizing all forms of human trafficking including labor exploitation, sexual exploitation, and organ trafficking. Defines aggravated offenses. Establishes the National Authority to Combat Trafficking in Persons (INLCTP). Provides victim protection, temporary residence permits for foreign trafficking victims, and rehabilitation assistance.
Criminal

Child Protection Code, Book III (Law No. 95-92 as amended)Juvenile Justice

Enacted: 1995 | Amended: 2002, 2008
Sets out a rehabilitative approach to juvenile delinquency: minors under 13 cannot be criminally prosecuted; those 13–17 may face protective measures or suspended sentences. Specialized Children's Courts presided over by a judge for children (juge des enfants). Diversion and rehabilitation preferred over incarceration. Placement in certified institutions possible.
CriminalPersonal Status

Organic Law No. 2017-10 of March 7, 2017 (Chapter V)Whistleblower Protections — Denunciation of Corruption

Enacted: March 7, 2017 | In force: March 2017 | Amendment bill pending since 2025
Chapter V of the Organic Law on the Good Governance and the Fight against Corruption establishes the first comprehensive whistleblower protection regime in Tunisia. Grants legal immunity and confidentiality to persons who report in good faith acts of corruption, embezzlement, or misuse of public funds to the national anti-corruption authority (INLUCC). Prohibits retaliation, demotion, or dismissal against public or private sector employees who report. Provides for witness protection measures in serious cases. Pending reform: a 42-article draft amendment, submitted by 15 MPs and under review by the ARP's Administration, Governance and Anti-Corruption Committee as of 2026, would more precisely define reporting procedures and protection timelines and create a new specialized anti-corruption body to centralize complaints and coordinate protective measures — filling the institutional gap left by INLUCC's effective sidelining since 2021 (see the Declaration of Assets entry). Not yet enacted.
Key provisions
  • Art. 44: Definition of protected disclosure in good faith
  • Art. 45: Prohibition of reprisals; reinstatement remedy
  • Art. 46: Confidentiality of reporter's identity
  • Art. 47: Criminal immunity for reporter (no prosecution for breach of professional secrecy)
  • Art. 48: INLUCC's duty to notify reporter of follow-up within 90 days
CriminalAdministrative

Law No. 2001-52 of May 14, 2001 (as amended)Penitentiary and Reform Law

Enacted: May 14, 2001 | Amended: 2016 (Decree-Law on probation)
Governs the organization of prisons, the treatment of detainees and convicted persons, and the conditions of detention in Tunisia. Establishes the rights of prisoners (medical care, education, family visits) and the duties of prison staff. A 2016 reform introduced electronic monitoring bracelets as an alternative measure for non-violent offenders, and expanded early-release (parole) conditions to ease prison overcrowding.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–10: Organization of the prison service under the Ministry of Justice
  • Art. 11–30: Classification of detainees (pre-trial vs. sentenced)
  • Art. 31–50: Rights of prisoners — healthcare, education, family contact
  • Art. 51–70: Work and vocational training for sentenced prisoners
  • Art. 71–85: Disciplinary regime and sanctions within prisons
  • 2016 addition: Electronic monitoring bracelet (bracelet électronique) as alternative sentence
CriminalAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 74-20 of October 24, 1974 (reform bill pending, 2026)Games of Chance & Gambling

Baseline enacted: October 24, 1974 (ratified by Law No. 74-96 of December 11, 1974) | Reform bill filed: January 19, 2026 | Referred to committee: January 29, 2026 | Not yet enacted
Tunisia's baseline gambling statute, Decree-Law No. 74-20 of October 24, 1974 (on itinerant installations, parlor games, and lotteries), bans unlicensed games of pure chance and lotteries under decades-old, minor penalties; a companion Decree-Law No. 74-21 of the same date separately authorizes licensed tourist casinos. A group of 23 Assembly of People's Representatives deputies filed a proposition de loi on January 19, 2026, referred to the Commission de la Législation Générale on January 29, 2026, to broaden the definition of "jeux de hasard" to explicitly cover online and digital gambling, criminalize unlicensed online betting, and require internet service providers and payment processors to block access to, and freeze flows toward, unlicensed offshore gambling platforms. A separate, government-drafted bill on sports-betting oversight — developed by the Ministry of Youth and Sports (Minister Sadok Mourali) to align with international anti-money-laundering standards — has been sent to the Head of Government's office but as of mid-2026 remains awaiting Council of Ministers review, not yet before Parliament. Neither reform has been enacted as of July 2026; the 1974 framework remains in force. Note: some press reports cite specific penalty figures for the pending bill (prison terms, fine amounts); these could not be independently corroborated against a primary text and are omitted here.
CriminalDigital

Organic Law No. 2018-50 of October 23, 2018Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination

Adopted by the ARP: October 9, 2018 | Promulgated: October 23, 2018 | In force: 2018
Landmark statute making Tunisia the first country in the Middle East and North Africa to criminalize racial discrimination. Article 2 defines racial discrimination broadly as any distinction, exclusion, restriction, or preference based on race, color, descent, or national or ethnic origin that impairs the equal enjoyment of rights and freedoms, aligning domestic law with Tunisia's obligations under the International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination (ratified 1967). Criminalizes discriminatory acts, incitement to racial hatred, and public glorification of racial discrimination, with enhanced penalties for organized or repeated offenses; legal entities that promote racial discrimination face fines. Article 11 establishes a National Commission against Racial Discrimination, attached to the ministry responsible for human rights, tasked with data collection, monitoring, and policy proposals. Implementation note (2024–2026): civil society and international observers (EuroMed Rights, MERIP, Arab Reform Initiative) describe the law's practical enforcement as limited — few prosecutions have been brought under it directly — while Saadia Mosbah, president of the prominent anti-racism organization Mnemty, was instead prosecuted on unrelated money-laundering and "illicit enrichment" charges (May 2024 arrest; 8-year sentence upheld on appeal, June 2026), a case Amnesty International and other rights groups characterize as the weaponization of ordinary criminal law against a leading advocate for the rights this statute was meant to protect.
CriminalConstitutionalSocial

🏪 Commercial Law

Commercial Code — Law No. 59-129 of October 5, 1959Majallat Al-Tijara

Enacted: October 5, 1959 | In force: January 1, 1960 | Amended: multiple times (through 2024)
Core statute governing commercial activities, traders, and commercial acts in Tunisia. Defines the status of traders (commerçants), regulates commercial registers, commercial agencies, and trade names. Covers bills of exchange, promissory notes, cheques, and commercial contracts. Has been substantially amended since 1960 to modernize commercial law, most recently by Law No. 2024-41 (see separate entry) which overhauled the cheque provisions (Arts. 410 et seq.).
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–15: Definition of commercial acts and traders
  • Art. 16–30: Commercial register obligations
  • Art. 57–90: Agency and commercial representation
  • Art. 192–260: Bills of exchange (lettres de change)
  • Art. 261–290: Promissory notes (billets à ordre)
  • Art. 291–325, 410 et seq.: Cheques — form, endorsement, payment, protest (cheque-specific rules substantially rewritten by Law No. 2024-41, effective February 2, 2025)
  • Art. 400+: Commercial leases (fonds de commerce)
CommercialAmended

Commercial Register Law — Law No. 95-44 of May 2, 1995Registre du Commerce (Run 54 addition)

Enacted: May 2, 1995 | Published: JORT No. 37, May 9, 1995 | Amended: Law No. 2010-15 of April 14, 2010
Standalone statute organizing the National Commercial Register, repealing the protectorate-era 1926 decree that first created it — distinct from, and more detailed than, the general registration obligations in Arts. 16–30 of the 1959 Commercial Code above. Requires all traders and commercial companies to register; mandates filings for incorporation, capital changes, management changes, and dissolution; and organizes the register's central and local offices under the Ministry of Industry and Trade. The 2010 amendment strengthened data-sharing between the register and other administrations. Wholly absent from this artifact despite 53 prior research runs.
CommercialAdministrative

Law No. 2024-41 of August 2, 2024Reform of Bad-Cheque Rules (amending Commercial Code Arts. 410 et seq.)

Published in JORT No. 94: August 2, 2024 | Entered into force: February 2, 2025
Major reform of the bounced-cheque ("chèque sans provision") regime, widely described as a turning point given Tunisia's historically high rate of check-related criminal prosecutions. Shifts the system toward bank-side solvency screening, a centralized verification platform, and partial decriminalization for low-value cheques.
Key provisions
  • Banks must assess a customer's solvency before issuing a chequebook and set a global ceiling and validity period (max. 6 months) per chequebook based on financial capacity
  • Maximum face value capped at 30,000 TND per cheque leaf
  • Decriminalizes issuing a bad cheque for amounts ≤ 5,000 TND — recourse becomes a civil/payment-order matter unless the beneficiary initiates proceedings
  • Criminal liability retained for cheques exceeding the 5,000 TND threshold, fraud, or bad faith
  • Establishes the Central Electronic Cheque Platform (PECC), operated by the Central Bank of Tunisia, allowing real-time verification and reservation of available funds by beneficiaries
Update (Run 40): Central Bank of Tunisia payment-systems data published June 7, 2026 shows the reform's real-world effect: cheque volume fell from 12.25 million (2024) to 4.07 million (2025) — cited by the Justice Minister as roughly a two-thirds drop — with businesses substituting bills of exchange (lettres de change), which rose 35.9% in volume (1.2 million units) and 23.5% in value (TND 13.9 billion) in Q1 2026 alone, alongside strong growth in mobile and e-payments. (Run 53): Human Rights Watch's June 10, 2024 report "No Way Out: Debt Imprisonment in Tunisia" — published before this reform took effect — documented 496 officially confirmed (and up to ~7,200 business-association-estimated) people imprisoned for unpaid cheques, framing pre-reform practice as a disguised debt-imprisonment regime; see the new Debt-Recovery Companies entry below for the wider debt-collection framework this reform sits alongside.
CommercialAmended

Companies Code — Law No. 2000-93 of November 3, 2000Majallat Al-Sharikaat

Enacted: November 3, 2000 | In force: 2001 | Amended: 2002, 2005, 2009, 2016, 2019
Comprehensive statute regulating all forms of companies in Tunisia. Governs formation, management, operation, auditing, transformation, merger, and dissolution of companies. Covers: SARL (private limited company), SA (joint-stock company), SNC (general partnership), SCS (limited partnership), and cooperative societies.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–50: General provisions applicable to all companies
  • Art. 51–128: Joint-stock companies (Sociétés Anonymes — SA): board of directors, general meetings, auditors
  • Art. 129–165: Private limited companies (SARL): capital minimum, management
  • Art. 166–200: General partnerships (SNC)
  • Art. 201–230: Limited partnerships (SCS, SCA)
  • Mandatory: external statutory auditor (commissaire aux comptes) for SA
  • Minority shareholder protection provisions
  • 2019 amendment: introduced single-member SARL (SUARL)
CommercialAmended

Collective Proceedings Code — Law No. 1995-34 of April 17, 1995Insolvency and Business Recovery

Enacted: April 17, 1995 | In force: 1995 | Amended: 1999, 2003, 2016
Governs insolvency, bankruptcy, judicial reorganization (redressement judiciaire), and liquidation of insolvent businesses. Establishes procedures for appointing judicial administrators and liquidators, protecting creditors, and rehabilitating viable enterprises. Amended in 2016 to expand preventive settlement mechanisms.
Key provisions
  • Preventive settlement (règlement amiable) for pre-insolvency situations
  • Judicial recovery (redressement judiciaire): 6-month observation period
  • Liquidation (faillite): asset realization and creditor distribution
  • Ranking of creditors: secured, preferred, unsecured
  • Art. 36: Suspension of creditor actions during reorganization
CommercialAmended

Consumer Protection Law — Law No. 92-117 of December 7, 1992

Enacted: December 7, 1992 | In force: 1993 | Amended: 2009, 2015
Protects consumers against defective goods, unfair commercial practices, misleading advertising, and price manipulation. Establishes the right to information, right to safety, and right to redress. Regulates consumer contracts including distance contracts and credit. Creates the National Council for Consumer Protection.
CommercialCivil

Draft Law No. 113/2020 (adopted by Council of Ministers, never promulgated)Consumer Credit & Household Over-indebtedness

Adopted by Council of Ministers: December 2020 | Status: Never promulgated; revived debate 2026
Tunisia has no dedicated statute defining household over-indebtedness or regulating consumer-credit affordability; a bill (referenced in secondary sources as "Loi n° 113/2020") addressing both was approved by the Council of Ministers in December 2020 but was never submitted to, or adopted by, parliament, leaving a persistent legal vacuum — no over-indebtedness observatory and no statutory debt-to-income caps. Correction: credit-bureau infrastructure specifically is no longer entirely absent — see the separate Credit Reporting (Credit Bureau) Decree-Law entry below, a narrower licensing framework that does not itself address affordability caps or over-indebtedness. Household debt reached an estimated TND 32.2 billion in 2024 (91% bank-issued, 9% microfinance), with a household debt-service burden reported to have risen from 107% to 171% of disposable income between 2014 and 2024. In March 2026 the Institut Arabe des Chefs d'Entreprises (IACE) published a policy note ("Le surendettement des ménages : une urgence nationale") renewing calls to promulgate the dormant bill and proposing debt-to-income caps (40% for consumer credit, 50% for mortgage credit) plus governorate-level over-indebtedness commissions offering a 7-year repayment plan, partial debt write-off with reintegration commitments, or full write-off paired with a 3-year credit ban. As of mid-2026 no such law had been enacted. Note: the "113/2020" bill number is drawn from secondary press/policy sources, not independently verified against a primary JORT or ARP record.
CommercialFinancialSocial

Decree-Law No. 2022-2 of January 4, 2022Credit Reporting (Credit Bureau) Licensing

Enacted: January 4, 2022 | Published: JORT N°2, January 5, 2022 | BCT implementing circular: No. 2022-09 of October 25, 2022
Creates a licensing ("agrément") regime, under Central Bank of Tunisia (BCT) supervision, for private "sociétés d'information sur le crédit" (credit-reporting companies/credit bureaus) — a narrower measure than, and distinct from, the still-unenacted household over-indebtedness bill above; it does not itself impose affordability caps or debt-to-income limits. Article 7 licensing criteria cover shareholder quality, technical/IT capacity, management fitness, governance, and personal-data-protection compliance (cross-referencing Law No. 2004-63). One firm, MITIGAN CIB, was initially licensed, but the BCT withdrew its agrément on March 17, 2026 for failing to launch operations within the required timeframe — leaving Tunisia with no operating licensed credit bureau as of mid-2026 despite the legal framework being in force since 2022.
FinancialDigital

Law No. 98-4 of February 2, 1998Debt-Recovery Companies

Enacted: February 2, 1998 | Amended: Law No. 2001-91 of August 7, 2001; Law No. 2003-42 of June 9, 2003
Regulates "sociétés de recouvrement de créances" (third-party debt-collection companies), setting minimum capital, licensing conditions, and permissible collection practices; Law No. 2001-91 and Law No. 2003-42 subsequently amended aspects of the regime. Actual debt-collection litigation runs through ordinary civil process under the Code of Obligations and Contracts and Code of Civil and Commercial Procedure. Separately, Tunisia's Commercial Code criminalizes issuing a bad cheque (see Law No. 2024-41 above), which Human Rights Watch's June 10, 2024 report "No Way Out: Debt Imprisonment in Tunisia" documented as a de facto debt-imprisonment regime — citing official figures of 496 people imprisoned for unpaid cheques and business-association estimates as high as ~7,200, and arguing the practice is inconsistent with ICCPR Article 11's prohibition on imprisonment for inability to fulfill a contractual obligation.
FinancialCivil

Competition and Prices Law — Law No. 2015-36 of September 15, 2015

Enacted: September 15, 2015 | In force: 2015 | Replaced: Law No. 91-64
Governs competition policy, prohibits anti-competitive agreements, abuse of dominant position, and anti-competitive mergers. Establishes the Competition Council (Conseil de la Concurrence) as independent regulatory authority. Regulates prices for essential goods and services. Pending reform: the Competition Council's president announced in April 2025 a planned "radical revision" of the law to align it with COMESA competition standards; as of mid-2026 this remained in a preparatory/consultative phase with no bill text yet before the ARP.
Commercial

Electronic Commerce Law — Law No. 2000-83 of August 9, 2000

Enacted: August 9, 2000 | In force: 2000 | Amended: 2004
First statute to legally recognize electronic contracts and digital signatures in Tunisia. Establishes the legal equivalence of electronic documents and handwritten signatures. Governs online commercial transactions, electronic payment, and online offers. Establishes the legal framework for certification authorities issuing digital certificates.

Law No. 94-89 of July 26, 1994Financial Leasing Law

Enacted: July 26, 1994 | In force: 1994
Regulates financial leasing (crédit-bail / ijar) of movable and immovable assets. Defines the leasing contract, rights and obligations of lessor and lessee, and end-of-term purchase options. Financial leasing companies must be licensed by the BCT. Key instrument for SME financing in Tunisia.

Law No. 95-87 of October 30, 1995SICAR Law

Enacted: October 30, 1995 | In force: 1995
Establishes the legal framework for Venture Capital Investment Companies (SICAR — Sociétés d'Investissement à Capital Risque). SICARs invest in the capital of SMEs and startups. Benefit from significant tax advantages: contributions to SICARs are deductible from taxable income. Regulated by the CMF (Financial Market Council).

Law No. 96-80 of October 8, 1996 (amending COC)Commercial Agents Law

Enacted: October 8, 1996 | In force: 1996
Regulates commercial agency agreements (contrats de représentation commerciale). Defines the rights and obligations of commercial agents (independent representatives). Provides mandatory compensation to agents on termination (indemnité de clientèle). Applied in cross-border distribution and agency arrangements.
Commercial

Decree-Law No. 2020-170 of November 10, 2020Crowdfunding Decree

Enacted: November 10, 2020 | In force: 2020
Establishes the legal framework for equity and loan-based crowdfunding platforms in Tunisia. Platforms require authorization from the CMF (equity crowdfunding) or BCT (loan crowdfunding). Maximum funding per project: 1,000,000 TND (loan) or 2,000,000 TND (equity). Enables startups under the Startup Act to raise equity via registered platforms.
CommercialDigitalFinancial

Law No. 2008-66 of October 20, 2008Factoring (Affacturage)

Enacted: October 20, 2008 | In force: January 2009
Establishes the legal framework for factoring operations (affacturage) in Tunisia. Authorizes banks and specialized financial institutions licensed by the Banque Centrale de Tunisie to provide factoring services — the purchase of trade receivables from commercial enterprises at a discount. Regulates the assignment of receivables to the factor, notification obligations to debtors, and the recourse/non-recourse models. Facilitates access to working capital for Tunisian exporters and domestic suppliers.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–5: Definition of factoring; licensed operators
  • Art. 6–12: Assignment of receivables — form and notification to debtors
  • Art. 13–18: Rights of the factor against debtor and seller
  • Art. 19–22: Recourse factoring vs. non-recourse factoring
  • Art. 23–27: BCT regulatory oversight; reporting obligations

👷 Labor Law

Labor Code — Law No. 66-27 of April 30, 1966Majallat Al-Shughl

Enacted: April 30, 1966 | In force: May 1966 | Amended: 1994, 1996, 2006, 2007, 2011, 2017
The primary statute governing employment relationships in Tunisia. Covers individual employment contracts, conditions of work, wages, termination, workplace safety, collective bargaining, and trade unions. Applies to the private sector; public servants are governed by the General Statute of Civil Servants. Implements ILO core labor conventions (Tunisia has ratified 62 ILO conventions).
Key provisions
  • Art. 6: Employment contract — types (fixed-term, indefinite-term, project-based)
  • Art. 14–22: Trial period (période d'essai)
  • Art. 23–35: Obligations of employer and employee
  • Art. 40: Minimum wage (SMIG/SMAG) — set by decree
  • Art. 73–83: Annual paid leave — minimum 1 day per month (18 days/year)
  • Art. 90–100: Work hours — 48 hours/week maximum (40 hours in industry)
  • Art. 120: Dismissal — requires just cause; procedural requirements for economic dismissal
  • Art. 123: Severance pay for unfair dismissal
  • Art. 152: Trade union rights; freedom of association
  • Art. 155: Right to strike (with pre-notice requirements)
  • Art. 193–200: Labor inspectorate powers
  • Art. 230–240: Occupational health and safety requirements
LaborAmended

General Statute of Civil Servants — Law No. 83-112 of December 12, 1983

Enacted: December 12, 1983 | In force: 1984 | Amended: 2007, 2011, 2017
Governs the employment conditions, rights, and obligations of Tunisian civil servants (fonctionnaires). Covers recruitment, grades, promotions, disciplinary procedures, leave, retirement, and termination. Complemented by specific statutes for particular sectors (teachers, judges, military, etc.).
LaborAdministrative

Social Security Law — Law No. 60-30 of December 14, 1960

Enacted: December 14, 1960 | In force: 1961 | Amended: extensively
Establishes the compulsory social security system. Organizes the National Social Security Fund (CNSS for private sector) and the National Retirement and Social Welfare Fund (CNRPS for public sector). Covers retirement pensions, invalidity, survivors' benefits, occupational accidents, and maternity allowances.
LaborSocial

Occupational Accidents and Diseases Law — Law No. 94-28 of February 21, 1994

Enacted: February 21, 1994 | In force: 1994
Defines occupational accidents and diseases, establishes mandatory employer liability, and sets compensation levels for temporary or permanent disability and death. Requires employers to report occupational accidents within 2 days. Organizes compulsory insurance through CNSS.
Labor

Law No. 2025-9 of May 21, 2025Employment Reform Law

Enacted: May 21, 2025 | Published: JORT May 23, 2025
Landmark reform overhauling Tunisia's employment framework. Three pillars: (1) Indefinite employment contracts (CDI) are the statutory default; CDDs (fixed-term contracts) permitted only in three circumstances: temporary workload increase, replacement of absent worker, and seasonal work; any non-compliant CDD is void and automatically converted to CDI with full seniority. (2) Prohibition of labor subcontracting — workers supplied via subcontracting arrangements are deemed permanent employees of the beneficiary company; employers have 3 months to regularize. (3) Criminal penalty of TND 10,000 fine for engaging prohibited subcontracting. Contested by employer federations; supported by UGTT.
Labor

Decrees No. 2026-63 through 2026-69 of April 30, 2026Public & Private Sector Wage Increase Package 2026–2028

Published: JORT No. 44, April 30, 2026 | Implements: Finance Law 2026 (Law No. 2025-17), Art. 15 | Effective: retroactive to January 1, 2026 | Covers: 2026–2028
A seven-decree package implementing Finance Law 2026 Art. 15 and Labor Code Art. 40, staging wage and pension increases across the entire public and private sectors over three years (2026–2028). Update (Run 38): previously this entry documented only the SMIG/SMAG/collective-agreement decrees (66–68); the package's other four decrees, covering the civil service, public enterprises, and the judiciary, are now added. Decree No. 2026-63 sets the raise program for agents of the state, local authorities, and administrative public establishments (fonctionnaires). Decree No. 2026-64 covers employees of public enterprises and establishments governed by special statutes or establishment-level collective agreements, staging monthly increases of TND 120 (cadres), TND 100–105 (supervisory/maîtrise), and TND 90 (execution-grade) across 2026–2028. Decree No. 2026-65 raises the "prime de magistrature" by TND 120/year (2026–2028) for judges of the judicial order, the Administrative Court, and the Court of Accounts, also extended to their retirement pensions. Decree No. 2026-66 sets the guaranteed agricultural minimum wage (SMAG) for workers 18 and over at TND 21.336/day in 2026, rising to TND 22.400 (2027) and TND 23.520 (2028), plus a technical-skill premium for specialized agricultural workers. Decree No. 2026-67 sets the guaranteed inter-professional minimum wage (SMIG) for non-agricultural sectors at TND 554.736/month (48-hour regime) or TND 470.251/month (40-hour regime) in 2026, rising in stages to TND 611.520 / TND 517.571 by 2028. Decree No. 2026-68 mandates an annual 5% increase in base salaries plus transport and attendance allowances for non-agricultural sectors covered by sectoral collective bargaining agreements, with monthly increases of roughly TND 36–46 for line workers, ~TND 53 for supervisory staff, and ~TND 69 for management-grade employees in 2026. Decree No. 2026-69 sets smaller, separately staged increases (roughly TND 30–39/month execution-grade, TND 44 maîtrise, TND 58–64 cadres in 2026, rising through 2028) for non-agricultural Labor Code employees covered by neither an enterprise-specific statute nor a sectoral collective agreement. A further schedule of monthly increases (TND 90–120, staged by category A1/A2/A3/B and phased through 2027–2028) applies to specified "sensitive" public-service corps, including health professionals (physicians, dentists, pharmacists, veterinarians), oversight/control bodies, scientific researchers, university academic staff, social-service personnel, and labor inspectors. Across all seven decrees, the increases extend to retirement pensions, are non-cumulative with certain pre-existing allowances, are excluded from year-end/performance-bonus calculations, and remain subject to standard social-security contributions; non-compliance with the SMIG/SMAG minimums is subject to the penalties under Art. 3 of the Labor Code Law of April 30, 1966.
LaborAdministrativeJudicial

Law No. 2024-44 of August 12, 2024Maternity and Paternity Leave Reform

Adopted by ARP: July 31, 2024 (111-1-4) | Published: JORT No. 2024-099, August 12, 2024
Restructures maternity and paternity leave across the public and private sectors. Expands paternity leave from the prior baseline of 1 day (private sector, Labor Code) / 2 days (civil servants) to 7 days at full pay — extended to 10 days for multiple births or a newborn with disability, prematurity, or a congenital condition requiring care, plus 3 additional days in the event of stillbirth; must be taken within 30 days of the birth. Restructures maternity leave into a prenatal component (up to 15 days in the final month, on medical order) and a postnatal component extended from 30 to 90 days (120 days for complicated or multiple births).
LaborSocialPersonal Status

Law No. 93-10 of February 17, 1993 (as amended)Vocational Training Law

Enacted: February 17, 1993 | In force: 1993 | Amended: multiple times
Framework law governing vocational and professional training in Tunisia. Creates the National Agency for Vocational Training (ATFP — Agence Tunisienne de la Formation Professionnelle). Establishes the national apprenticeship system, certification standards, and quality assurance for vocational qualifications. Employers must contribute 1% of payroll to the Vocational Training Fund (TFP).
LaborEducation

🏛️ Administrative Law

Administrative Court Law — Law No. 72-40 of June 1, 1972

Enacted: June 1, 1972 | In force: 1972 | Amended: multiple times
Establishes the Administrative Court (Tribunal Administratif) as the supreme administrative jurisdiction. Has jurisdiction over disputes between citizens and public bodies, annulment of illegal administrative acts, and administrative contracts. Also serves as the Supreme Audit Institution reviewing public accounts. Organized into chambers; appeals go to Administrative Court plenary.
Administrative

Local Government Code — Law No. 2018-29 of May 9, 2018Majallat Al-Jama'aat Al-Mahaliyya

Enacted: May 9, 2018 | In force: May 2018 | Implements: 2014 Constitution Chapter VII
Comprehensive decentralization legislation replacing the previous municipalities law. Organizes three levels of local government: municipalities (balladiyyat), regions (wilayat), and districts (aqalim). Establishes directly-elected municipal councils with legislative powers in local matters. Introduces participatory budgeting and citizens' right to petition. Implemented after 2018 municipal elections. Superseded in part: Organic Law No. 2025-4 of March 12, 2025 (JORT No. 30, March 13, 2025; adopted by the ARP February 27, 2025, 109–2–4) repealed this code's region and district provisions (and Organic Law No. 89-11 of February 4, 1989 on regional councils), replacing the elected-region model with local, regional, and district councils organized by decree, and transferring former regional councils' assets and holdings to the State for governors to administer. The 2018 code's municipal-level provisions remain in force; local council elections continue to be organized under Decree-Law No. 2023-10 of March 8, 2023 (see the CNRD entry in Constitutional Law).
Key provisions
  • Three levels: municipality, region (governorate), district
  • Directly elected councils at all three levels
  • Fiscal autonomy — own revenue sources and transfers from central budget
  • Participatory democracy mechanisms (citizen petitions, public consultation)
  • Gender parity requirement for municipal council lists
  • Youth quota (minimum 1/3 of each list under 35)
Administrative

Public Procurement Decree — Decree No. 2014-1039 of March 13, 2014

Enacted: March 13, 2014 | In force: 2014 | Amended: 2022
Governs all public procurement by state entities, public establishments, and local authorities. Establishes competitive bidding requirements, transparency obligations, evaluation criteria, and remedies for tenderers. Creates the National Commission for Public Procurement Investigations (HAICOP). Sets thresholds for direct award, restricted tendering, and open tendering.
Administrative

Access to Information Decree — Decree-Law No. 2011-41 of May 26, 2011

Enacted: May 26, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Strengthened by 2016 Organic Law
Grants citizens the right to access information held by public bodies. Establishes a 20-day response deadline for requests. Replaced in part by the more comprehensive Organic Law No. 2016-22 of March 24, 2016, which created an independent Access to Information Commission (IVD predecessor).
AdministrativePress

Law No. 2016-46 of July 11, 2016Electronic Procedures in Administrative Transactions

Enacted: July 11, 2016 | In force: 2017
Authorizes and regulates e-government services. Allows administrative procedures to be conducted electronically. Establishes standards for electronic authentication, digital archives, and online payment to the state. Part of Tunisia's e-government reform strategy.
AdministrativeDigital

Law No. 2021-35 of July 21, 2021Administrative Procedure Law

Enacted: July 21, 2021 | In force: 2021 | Partially implemented
Codifies and standardizes administrative procedures, notification requirements, and citizens' rights when dealing with public bodies. Establishes the "silence means approval" principle for certain administrative decisions (within 30 days). Strengthens citizens' right to be heard before adverse administrative decisions. Partially implemented pending regulatory decrees.
Administrative

Law No. 2017-58 addendum / Circular No. 39 of 2017Civil Service Reform

Enacted: 2017
Introduces performance management and results-based evaluation for civil servants. Creates management by objectives (MBO) framework for public entities. Reorganizes career tracks and introduces merit-based promotion criteria. Part of broader public administration reform supported by World Bank.
AdministrativeLabor

💰 Tax & Fiscal Law

Income Tax Code — Law No. 89-114 of December 30, 1989Code de l'Impôt sur le Revenu des Personnes Physiques et l'Impôt sur les Sociétés

Enacted: December 30, 1989 | In force: January 1, 1990 | Amended: annually in Finance Laws
Governs personal income tax (IRPP) and corporate income tax (IS) in Tunisia. Personal income tax is progressive with rates from 0% to 35%. Corporate income tax standard rate is 15%, with higher rates for banks and telecoms (35%) and reduced rates for SMEs and certain sectors. Amended annually through the Finance Law.
Key rates (as of 2024)
  • Personal income tax: 0% up to 5,000 TND, 26% bracket, 35% top rate
  • Corporate income tax: 15% standard rate
  • Banks, financial institutions, telecoms: 35%
  • SMEs: reduced rate of 10% (conditions apply)
  • Withholding tax on dividends: 10%
  • Capital gains tax: included in income tax
TaxAmended

VAT Code — Law No. 88-61 of June 2, 1988Code de la Taxe sur la Valeur Ajoutée

Enacted: June 2, 1988 | In force: January 1, 1989 | Amended: annually
Establishes value-added tax in Tunisia. Standard rate 19%; reduced rates of 13% (certain services) and 7% (basic goods, agricultural products). Exports are zero-rated. Registration required for businesses with turnover exceeding 100,000 TND/year (services) or 200,000 TND (goods).
TaxAmended

Tax Procedures Code — Law No. 2000-82 of August 9, 2000Code des Droits et Procédures Fiscaux

Enacted: August 9, 2000 | In force: 2001 | Amended: annually
Unifies and governs all tax administration procedures including filing obligations, tax audits, assessment, payment, penalties, and dispute resolution. Establishes taxpayers' rights during tax audits. Provides for administrative appeals before tax committees and judicial appeals before administrative courts.
Tax

Customs Code — Law No. 2008-77 of December 22, 2008

Enacted: December 22, 2008 | In force: 2009 | Replaced: 1955 Customs Code
Governs importation, exportation, transit, and customs duties. Establishes the legal framework for customs declarations, inspections, duty rates, and customs offenses. Aligns Tunisia's customs procedures with international standards (WCO Revised Kyoto Convention). Governs free zones and customs warehouses.
TaxCommercial

Local Taxes Law — Law No. 97-11 of February 3, 1997

Enacted: February 3, 1997 | In force: 1997 | Amended: multiple times
Regulates taxes and fees collected by municipalities including property taxes (TFP — taxe foncière sur les propriétés bâties et non bâties), tourism taxes, advertising taxes, and local business taxes (TCL — taxe sur les établissements à caractère industriel, commercial ou professionnel).
TaxAdministrative

Decree-Law No. 2022-79 of December 22, 2022Finance Law 2023

Enacted: December 22, 2022 | Published: JORT No. 141, December 23, 2022 | In force: January 1, 2023
Annual budget law, exceptionally issued as a presidential decree-law rather than an ordinary parliamentary statute because the Assembly of People's Representatives had not yet resumed full legislative activity following the December 2022 legislative elections. Set state expenditure at approximately TND 54 billion against TND 46.4 billion in revenue (deficit of approximately TND 7.5 billion). Introduced a real-estate wealth tax on individuals' property holdings worth TND 3 million or more, and temporary customs/VAT relief on EV charging equipment.
Tax

Law No. 2023-13 of December 11, 2023Finance Law 2024

Adopted by ARP: December 10, 2023 (116 for, 4 against, 26 abstentions) | Enacted: December 11, 2023 | Published: JORT, December 12, 2023 | Fiscal year: 2024
First annual Finance Law passed by the elected Assembly of People's Representatives since 2021. Key changes: Corporate Income Tax (CIT) increased from 15% to 20% for general companies effective January 1, 2024. Bank and insurance company CIT rate increased from 35% to 40%. New 2% conjunctural contribution on FY2023 taxable income for companies with turnover ≥ TND 20 million. Standard deduction for rental income raised from 20% to 25%. New 4-year CIT/IRPP exemption for enterprises filing investment declarations in 2024–2025. Raised import duties on solar panels from 10% to 30% effective January 1, 2025.
TaxAmended

Law No. 2024-48 of December 9, 2024Finance Law 2025

Adopted by ARP/CNRD: December 2 & 4, 2024 | Enacted: December 9, 2024 | Published: JORT No. 149, December 10, 2024 | Fiscal year: 2025
Key measures: VAT reduced to 7% (from 13%) on low-voltage electricity for domestic consumers using ≤300 kWh/month. New conjunctural budget contribution (2% on FY2024 taxable income) for large companies (turnover ≥ TND 20M). Additional tax incentives for R&D-intensive companies. Community enterprises exempt from VAT under new suspension regime. Reinforces tax inspection powers for real estate valuations. Raises low pensions to TND 260 with roughly 7–7.5% increases for retirees, plus income-tax relief for pensioners, and begins phasing in mandatory electronic invoicing for an expanded range of service providers.
TaxAmended

Law No. 2025-17 of December 12, 2025Finance Law 2026

Adopted: ARP December 4, 2025 (89 for/23 against/12 abstain); Council of Regions and Districts December 7, 2025 | Published: JORT No. 148, December 12, 2025 | Fiscal year: 2026
State budget sets revenues at TND 52.56 billion and expenditures at TND 63.575 billion, with tax receipts of TND 47.773 billion as the dominant funding source. Key measures: Art. 88 establishes a new annual "wealth tax" (impôt sur la fortune) on Tunisian-resident individuals' worldwide net patrimony (non-residents taxed only on Tunisian-situs assets, subject to double-tax treaties), at 0.5% on net wealth between TND 3–5 million and 1% above TND 5 million, assessed as of January 1 each year including minor children's assets held in trust; exempts the taxpayer's principal residence, professional assets recorded on a business balance sheet, home-savings and investment-savings accounts, life insurance/capitalization contracts, and non-utility vehicles rated ≤12 fiscal horsepower; declared electronically by end of June each year, first applying to the 2026 tax year; implementing guidance issued via Note Commune n°13 (DGELF, June 11, 2026) and n°15. Art. 56 expands mandatory e-invoicing from goods transactions to the entire services sector (~380,000 professionals affected), effective January 1, 2026 — invoices must be issued through the "El Fatoora" platform operated by Tunisie TradeNet (TTN) in the standardized TEIF/XML format and bear a qualified electronic signature issued by TunTrust/ANCE; penalties run 100–500 TND per invoice (capped at 50,000 TND/year) for invoices wrongly issued outside the electronic system where e-invoicing is mandatory, and 250–10,000 TND (doubled for repeat offenses) for non-compliant electronic invoices; tax incentives for new/used vehicle imports for resident families; state-subsidized interest bonuses (up to 3 points) on investment credits taken out in 2026–2027; salary and pension increases staged across 2026–2028 by decree; VAT exemption on packaging inputs for locally produced olive oil; VAT reduced to 7% on EV charging equipment and lithium battery manufacturing inputs; authorization for farmers to import and use agricultural drones. A parliamentary amendment adopted December 2, 2025 (69–17–17) additionally authorizes Tunisian residents to open and hold foreign-currency bank accounts for the first time — see Exchange Control Law entry. Tunisian financial-press sources (ilBoursa, InFirst Auditors) also report that the FY2024–2025 "conjunctural contribution" of 4% on taxable profit — previously limited to banks, financial establishments, and insurers (see the Finance Law 2024 entry) — was made permanent under this law and extended to telecommunications network operators and automobile dealers/concessionaires; this extension is corroborated by two independent secondary sources but the specific implementing article number could not be confirmed against a primary JORT text during this research pass, so it is noted here with that caveat.
TaxAmended

Proposition de Loi Organique n° 2023-013Statute of the Tax Adviser Profession

Filed: 2023 | ARP Commission de la Législation Générale review ongoing: article-by-article vote October 23, 2025; disputed-article sessions June 3 and June 18, 2026 | Not yet finalized | Not yet enacted
A deputy-initiated organic bill (proposition de loi organique n° 2023-013, also cited as 13/2023) to create and regulate a licensed "conseiller fiscal" (tax adviser) profession in Tunisia, defining qualification requirements, a professional order, and the rights and duties of practitioners advising on tax matters. Reviewed by the Assembly of People's Representatives' Commission de la Législation Générale since at least mid-2025; the Ministries of Finance and Justice raised objections concerning overlap with the Code des Droits et Procédures Fiscaux and existing rules governing the legal (lawyer) profession. The commission held an article-by-article vote on the full bill text on October 23, 2025, but a residual set of "disputed articles" (fasl khilafiya) remained unresolved. Correction: examination was not finalized by early June as previously tracked — a June 3, 2026 session (following an earlier study day) specifically reviewed those remaining disputed articles, and the commission resumed again on June 18, 2026 to continue that same review and set its forward work program; no finalization or vote has been confirmed since. Status as of July 2026: still in committee review, not yet scheduled for plenary, not yet adopted.
TaxAdministrative

🏦 Financial & Banking Law

Banking Law — Law No. 2016-48 of July 11, 2016

Enacted: July 11, 2016 | In force: 2016 | Replaced: Law No. 2001-65
Regulates credit institutions, banking activities, and financial services in Tunisia. Establishes licensing requirements for banks and microfinance institutions. Strengthens the supervisory powers of the Central Bank of Tunisia (BCT). Introduces new categories of banking licenses and regulates Islamic banking activities.
Key provisions
  • Licensing: exclusive reserve of banking activities for licensed credit institutions
  • Capital requirements: minimum TND 25 million for universal banks
  • Supervision: BCT has powers to inspect, sanction, and revoke licenses
  • Islamic banking: participatory banks regulated under specific provisions
  • Deposit guarantee: Fund for Deposit Guarantee created
  • Resolution: framework for failing banks established
Financial

Central Bank Law — Law No. 2016-35 of April 25, 2016

Enacted: April 25, 2016 | In force: 2016 | Replaced: Law No. 58-90 (1958) | Amended: 2024, 2026
Establishes the Banque Centrale de Tunisie (BCT) as an independent institution responsible for monetary policy, exchange rate management, payment systems oversight, and banking supervision. Grants operational independence to the BCT Governor. Article 25 originally prohibited direct central bank financing of the state budget deficit. This prohibition was exceptionally suspended by Law No. 2024-10 of February 7, 2024 (JORT No. 2024-021, adopted by the ARP 92 votes to 41), authorizing the BCT to lend the Treasury up to TND 7,000 million, interest-free, repayable over 10 years with a 3-year grace period, to help cover the 2024 budget deficit — a measure Carnegie Endowment and TIMEP analyses describe as eroding central bank independence. Update (Run 40): Finance Law 2026 Art. 12 authorizes a further, separate interest-free BCT advance to the Treasury of up to TND 11,000 million (up from TND 7,000 million in 2024/2025). Independent of fiscal financing, the BCT cut its policy rate in three steps from 8% to 7% between late 2025 and March 2026 (held at 7% at its March 30, 2026 meeting); a March 26, 2026 circular (No. 2026-4) newly restricts bank financing of "non-priority" imports, generally requiring 100% importer-funded deposits; and in January 2026 the BCT granted OFT Tunisie (Ooredoo's fintech arm) a definitive payment-institution license (JORT No. 65/2026).
FinancialAmended

Insurance Code — Law No. 92-24 of February 9, 1992

Enacted: February 9, 1992 | In force: 1992 | Amended: 1997, 2002, 2008, 2014
Governs all insurance and reinsurance activities in Tunisia. Mandatory insurance categories include third-party motor liability, employer liability, and professional liability for certain professions. Regulates insurance contract terms, claims procedures, and policyholder protections. Insurance companies supervised by the Insurance General Committee (CGA). Pending reform: on March 29, 2026, 14 ARP deputies filed a bill to raise road-accident compensation caps under the motor-liability provisions introduced by amending Law No. 2005-86: proposed indexation to 5× annual income for a surviving spouse, 10× the SMIG for victims without stable income, a 3-year SMIG supplement for breadwinners, and triennial inflation-linked revision of caps. Not yet enacted as of June 2026; the exact proposition number was not independently confirmed.
Financial

Microfinance Law — Law No. 2011-117 of November 5, 2011
