Provides for home-care assistance programs for elderly persons without family support. Prohibits abandonment of elderly persons; criminalizes neglect by legal guardians or responsible family members. The 2004 amendment strengthened quality control inspections of private care facilities.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–6: Definition of elderly person; recognition of family as primary caregiver
  • Art. 7–14: State obligation to provide homes for elderly without family support
  • Art. 15–20: Licensing requirements for public and private elderly care facilities
  • Art. 21–26: Minimum standards: hygiene, nutrition, medical care, social activity
  • Art. 27–32: Prohibition of abandonment; criminal sanctions (up to 2 years)
  • Art. 33–38: Home-care assistance programs financed by CNSS/public funds
Social

Decree-Law No. 2011-97 of October 24, 2011Martyrs and Wounded of the Revolution

Enacted: October 24, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Amended: Law No. 2012-26 of December 24, 2012
Establishes benefits, pensions, and priority employment/healthcare rights for the families of persons killed and for those wounded during the 2010–2011 revolution. The 2012 amendment broadened eligibility criteria and revised the composition of the commission responsible for determining beneficiary status. Distinct from the later Transitional Justice Law (Organic Law No. 2013-53, see Judicial Organization section), which addresses truth-seeking and accountability for a much longer historical period rather than direct revolution-era victim benefits.
SocialAdministrative

🏠 Land & Property Law

Real Property Registration — Law No. 65-5 of February 12, 1965 (as reformed)

Origin: 1885 (protectorate) | Major overhaul: Law No. 65-5 of February 12, 1965 | Amended: 1992, 2005
Governs land title registration (immatriculation foncière) through the Property Rights Registry (Conservation de la Propriété Foncière). Once registered, title is conclusive and indefeasible. Unregistered land may be subject to adverse possession claims. Tunisia operates a Torrens-style title system for registered properties.
LandCivil

Urban Planning Code — Law No. 94-122 of November 28, 1994

Enacted: November 28, 1994 | In force: 1995 | Amended: 2003, 2009, 2016
Governs land use planning, building permits, zoning, and urban development in Tunisia. Requires detailed urban plans (PAD — Plans d'Aménagement de Détail) for urban areas. Establishes building permit requirements, setback rules, and height restrictions. Regulates real estate development and subdivision approvals. Pending reform (Run 45): the ARP's Strategic Planning/Urban Planning Committee approved, by majority vote and in amended form, a bill reactivating and updating the separate construction-regularization statute (Law No. 2015-11) to let owners of non-compliant buildings regularize their status and obtain utility-hookup authorizations; reported June 23, 2026, the bill was headed toward a committee report ahead of a plenary vote, though its ARP registry number could not be independently confirmed and is omitted pending a primary-source citation. Detail added (Run 49): per committee-hearing coverage from earlier in its progress (the bill was first heard by the same committee on February 27, 2026, before its June 23 amended-form approval), the proposal comprises 12 articles setting out eligible categories, land-registration valuation, and the procedures for benefiting from regularization; it targets housing built without authorization or in violation of planning rules before a December 31, 2025 cutoff, with sponsors citing estimates of roughly 800,000 to one million residents across some 1,400 informal ("anarchic") neighborhoods nationwide as potential beneficiaries. Post-cutoff violations would face demolition or a fine of up to three times the land's value. These figures come from pre-committee-vote reporting (businessnews.com.tn, La Presse, African Manager) on what appears to be the same proposal tracked since Run 45 rather than a primary ARP/JORT text, and the registry number remains unconfirmed — treat the article count and beneficiary estimates as provisional until a post-vote committee report or JORT text is located.
LandAdministrative

Housing Law — Law No. 77-46 of May 30, 1977

Enacted: May 30, 1977 | In force: 1977 | Amended: 1996, 2013
Governs residential leases and tenant-landlord relations. Sets minimum lease terms, notice periods for termination, and tenant protection against arbitrary eviction. Regulates rent increases for controlled accommodation. Applies to residential leases; commercial leases are regulated separately under the COC.
LandCivil

Co-ownership Law — Law No. 77-42 of May 23, 1977

Enacted: May 23, 1977 | In force: 1977 | Amended: 1994
Governs horizontal co-ownership (apartment buildings and condominiums). Defines common areas vs. private lots, establishes the mandatory Syndicate of Co-owners (syndicat des copropriétaires), and regulates management, maintenance costs, and dispute resolution between co-owners.
LandCivil

Habous (Religious Endowments) — Decree of July 18, 1956 (as reformed)

Reform Decree: July 18, 1956 | Multiple administrative reforms since
Habous (Arabic: وقف — waqf) are religious endowments inalienably dedicated to Islamic charitable purposes. The 1956 decree reorganized habous administration under the Ministry of Religious Affairs. Public habous were progressively converted to state property for national development projects. Private habous recognized but creation of new ones restricted. Habous property cannot be sold, mortgaged, or partitioned. A historically significant category of Tunisian real property, particularly urban commercial and agricultural land.
LandCivil

Law No. 83-87 of November 11, 1983 and Law No. 2001-29 of March 19, 2001Agricultural Land Protection

Enacted: 1983, 2001 | In force: ongoing
Restricts conversion of agricultural land to non-agricultural uses. Prime agricultural land (irrigated perimeters, fertile coastal plains) protected from subdivision and urbanization without prior Ministry of Agriculture approval. The 2001 law governs management of communal pastoral lands (terres collectives): partitioning, titling, and integration into the formal land registry. Essential to food security policy.
LandAgriculture

Law No. 69-56 of September 22, 1969Restriction on Foreign Ownership of Agricultural Land

Enacted: September 22, 1969 | In force: 1969 | Status: in force, subject to pending reform proposals (2025–2026)
Reserves ownership of agricultural land to persons of Tunisian nationality, agricultural cooperatives, and public legal entities (Art. 1); foreign nationals may not acquire agricultural property, a policy framed around food-security and productive-land-resource protection. Foreigners may lease agricultural land, generally capped at short renewable terms absent special authorization. As of 2025–2026 the rule on outright ownership is unchanged, but it is being debated alongside two related, separately pending legislative proposals on state-owned ("domanial") agricultural land: Proposal No. 32/2025, examined by the ARP Agriculture Committee in February 2026, would regularize and reallocate already-exploited state land — granting title to long-term informal farmers who developed neglected state plots over decades — while a second track examines long-term leasing (rather than ownership transfer) of state land to investors, including foreign investors, without altering the 1969 ownership restriction. Neither proposal had been adopted as of June 2026.
LandAgriculture

Law No. 2016-53 of July 11, 2016Expropriation for Public Utility

Enacted: July 11, 2016 | Amended: Decree-Law No. 2022-65 of October 19, 2022 (JORT No. 114, Oct. 21, 2022) | Further revision under study since Dec. 2024
Governs expropriation of private property for public utility (utilité publique), replacing the older 1976 expropriation code. Sets the procedure for declaring a project of public interest, valuing affected property, and compensating owners, with disputes heard before the Expropriation Court. Decree-Law No. 2022-65 amended the law to create a permanent "commission de reconnaissance et de conciliation" (recognition and conciliation commission) in each governorate to speed up amicable compensation settlements. A December 16, 2024 working session chaired by the Minister of State Domains and Land Affairs, Wajdi Hedhili — held pursuant to Circular No. 27 of the Presidency of Government (November 7, 2024) on accelerating public projects — launched study of a further revision intended to speed up expropriation procedures while safeguarding owners' compensation rights; the revision remained at the project/consultation stage, not yet enacted, as of mid-2026.
LandAdministrative

🏢 Associations & Civil Society

Decree-Law No. 2011-88 of September 24, 2011Associations and Non-Profit Organizations

Enacted: September 24, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Replaced: Law No. 59-154 (1959)
Post-revolution statute liberalizing civil society formation. Replaced the restrictive 1959 law. Article 12: associations are legally constituted upon submission of a notification letter — no prior government approval required. Prohibits dissolution by executive action without judicial order. Enabled a rapid expansion of civil society: over 20,000 associations registered by 2015. Pending reform, updated status: a draft organic law replacing this decree-law (originally filed October 2023) is under active examination by the ARP Rights & Freedoms Commission — not merely "with the government" as previously reported — with the parliamentary bureau setting a June 24, 2026 deadline for the commission's report; as of early June 2026 the commission was still awaiting requested government agencies' opinions. Commission chair Thabet El Abed described the amended bill's foreign-funding provisions as a "disguised authorization dressed as a declaration," tightening administrative control (via Ministries of Interior and Foreign Affairs oversight and mandatory reporting of foreign transfers to the CTAF) over associational autonomy. At its June 3, 2026 session the commission was reportedly still working from a newly-received, updated version of the bill text requiring further review, had received no official government responses to its consultation letters, and had not yet scheduled civil-society hearings (Al Chourouk, June 4, 2026) — all of which suggests the June 24 report deadline was more likely missed than met, though no independent reporting has surfaced confirming whether the commission actually submitted a report by that date or what it concluded. This outcome remains unverified and should be checked again as later coverage emerges. On May 22, 2026, commission member Fatma Mseddi sent formal correspondence to the commission's president describing the file — by then covering at least twelve hearing sessions plus a dedicated study day — as effectively "blocked" despite having reached what she called an "advanced" stage, and pressed the ARP not to delay it further; this predates, and is consistent with, the June 24 deadline subsequently slipping without a confirmed outcome. Update (Run 34): as of July 1, 2026 the bill remains alive, not abandoned — the Rights & Freedoms Commission is now studying it jointly with the pending nationality-code and personal-data-protection bills (webdo.tn), with the commission publicly calling for "large concertation avec les acteurs concernés" (broad consultation with concerned stakeholders) before any adoption; no report or vote has yet followed the lapsed June 24 deadline. Civil society groups continue to warn the reform would restrict freedoms; not yet enacted as of July 2, 2026.
Key provisions
  • Art. 3: Freedom to form associations guaranteed
  • Art. 10: Founders submit notification (not seek prior approval) to the Secretary General of Government
  • Art. 12: Association acquires legal personality upon notification
  • Art. 14: Registration certificate issued within 7 days
  • Art. 26: Dissolution only by judicial order on defined grounds
  • Art. 31: Foreign associations may operate with prior authorization
AdministrativeConstitutional

Decree-Law No. 2011-87 of September 24, 2011Political Parties

Enacted: September 24, 2011 | In force: 2011 | Amended: 2014
Governs the formation, registration, financing, and activities of political parties. Requires notification-based registration (not prior authorization). Prohibits parties based exclusively on religion, race, gender, or region. Mandates financial transparency and annual reporting. Political party financing regulated: prohibits foreign funding, limits campaign spending.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Organic Law No. 2014-16 of May 26, 2014 (as amended)Electoral Law

Enacted: May 26, 2014 | In force: 2014 | Amended: 2019, 2022, September 27, 2024
Governs elections for the President of the Republic, the Assembly of People's Representatives, and the Assembly of Regions and Districts. Establishes the Independent High Authority for Elections (ISIE) as the permanent electoral commission. Sets rules on candidacy, campaigning, vote counting, and dispute resolution. The 2022 amendments (Decree-Law 2022-22) modified ISIE membership and reduced its independence according to critics. September 27, 2024 amendment: adopted by the ARP just nine days before the October 6, 2024 presidential election, it transferred jurisdiction over electoral disputes — including candidate-eligibility appeals previously heard by the Administrative Court — to the ordinary Court of Appeal of Tunis, with a 48-hour deadline to file contested-result challenges. The change followed Administrative Court rulings reinstating several candidates ISIE had excluded, and was criticized by Human Rights Watch and other observers as weakening judicial checks on election integrity at a sensitive moment.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Draft Law No. 027/2023 and Government Counter-Draft (2024)Proposed NGO Reform (Pending)

Parliamentary draft introduced: 2023 | Government draft reported: July 2024 | New organic law proposal: April 2026 | Status: Not enacted as of July 2026
Several competing proposals to replace Decree-Law No. 2011-88 on associations. The most advanced, presented to the ARP in April 2026, is a comprehensive organic law proposal of 45 articles across 8 chapters governing the creation, management, financing, oversight, and sanctioning of associations. Its Article 26 would ban all foreign funding of associations without prior written authorization from the Prime Minister's office (Présidence du Gouvernement), with a 2-month response deadline and implicit approval if the deadline lapses unanswered. The ARP held a dedicated study day on the bill in April 2026, and its Rights and Freedoms Committee was tasked with finalizing its report by June 24, 2026. Strongly opposed by Tunisian civil society, Article 19, ICNL, and Amnesty International as reversing the 2011 notification-based system. As of July 2026, no final law adopted; the 2011 Decree-Law remains formally in force, though see the entry below on its administrative circumvention in practice.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Proposition de loi organique (May 2026)Unified Electoral Code Proposal

Presented: May 10, 2026 (initial proposal); May 19, 2026 (expanded coverage) | Status: Under committee review, not enacted
A group of 29 Assembly of People's Representatives deputies presented an organic law proposal to consolidate Tunisia's electoral and referendum rules — currently scattered across the base 2014 Electoral Law and its many subsequent amending organic laws and decree-laws (including the 2022 ISIE reorganization and the September 27, 2024 dispute-jurisdiction amendment already documented above) — into a single unified electoral code. Transmitted to the ARP's Committee on Internal Rules, Electoral Laws, and Parliamentary Functions on May 7, 2026. As drafted, the code runs to 261 articles across three parts: (1) rules for direct elections (presidential, legislative) and referendums; (2) rules for regional/district council elections and the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts; and (3) transitional and final provisions. The proposal would centralize rules governing presidential, legislative, municipal, local, and regional elections plus referendums in one text, revise the distribution of electoral constituencies, address vacant-seat procedures in overseas electoral districts, and clarify the relationship between candidates and the Court of Accounts on campaign-finance disputes. A May 19, 2026 committee session opened debate on two contested provisions carried over from earlier electoral-law drafts: a proposed 5,000-dinar candidacy deposit (caution) and a minimum education-level requirement for candidates, both described in Tunisian press coverage as a "sensitive debate" among deputies. Distinct from, and broader in scope than, the narrower September 2024 amendment; as of July 2026 it remains under committee examination with no vote scheduled.
ConstitutionalAdministrative

Administrative Suspensions of Civil Society OrganizationsLTDH, ASF Tunisia, ATFD & FTDES (2025–2026)

ATFD & FTDES measures: 2025 | LTDH suspension: notified April 24, 2026, for one month | Avocats Sans Frontières Tunisia suspension: notified May 5, 2026 | LTDH court appeal: postponed June 10, 2026, to July 15, 2026
Rather than amending Decree-Law 2011-88 itself, authorities have increasingly relied on administrative and fiscal powers to suspend prominent civil society organizations' activities. In April 2026, the Ligue Tunisienne pour la Défense des Droits de l'Homme (LTDH) — founded 1976, one of the oldest human rights organizations in Africa and the Arab world, and a member of the National Dialogue Quartet awarded the 2015 Nobel Peace Prize — was formally notified of a one-month suspension of its activities, officially justified on grounds of regularizing its fiscal and administrative situation. LTDH filed a court appeal against the suspension; the competent chamber of the Tunis Court of First Instance held a hearing on June 10, 2026 but postponed examination of the appeal to July 15, 2026, leaving the matter unresolved as of this update. Days later, in May 2026, Avocats Sans Frontières Tunisia (ASF Tunisia) was notified of a comparable suspension. The Tunisian Association of Democratic Women (ATFD) and the Tunisian Forum for Economic and Social Rights (FTDES) faced comparable measures during 2025, part of a wave reported by rights groups as exceeding 20 NGO suspensions since July 2025. Decree-Law 2011-88, Art. 26 requires dissolution to occur only by judicial order; rights groups (FIDH, Committee for Justice, SNJT) characterize these administrative suspensions as circumventing that judicial-order requirement and warn they set a precedent for sidelining the 2011 framework without formally repealing it. Update (Run 39): Amnesty International (May 2026) and Human Rights Watch (June 9, 2026) reports independently put the wider tally at roughly 25 associations affected by suspension or comparable administrative obstruction since mid-2025, and name additional organizations beyond the four detailed above, including Aswat Nissa, the World Organisation Against Torture's Tunisia programme (OMCT-Tunisia), I Watch (the Tunisian chapter of Transparency International), and the investigative outlet Nawaat. Both organizations frame the pattern as a coordinated use of fiscal-regularization and administrative-authorization pretexts against rights, anti-corruption, and media-adjacent groups specifically, rather than an across-the-board enforcement sweep. This remains executive/judicial practice under the existing Decree-Law 2011-88, distinct from the still-pending organic-law reform of that decree tracked in the entry above.
AdministrativeConstitutional

🛡️ Security & Counter-terrorism

Organic Law No. 2015-26 of August 7, 2015Countering Terrorism and Suppression of Money Laundering (as amended)

Enacted: August 7, 2015 (corrected from a previously listed July 7, 2015) | Amended: Organic Law No. 2019-9 of January 23, 2019
Cross-referenced from the Criminal Law section (see the fuller entry there for provisions and the 2019-9 amendment). Comprehensive counter-terrorism statute enacted following the Bardo Museum and Sousse beach attacks (2015). Replaced the 2003 anti-terrorism law. Defines terrorism broadly, criminalizes financing, propaganda, recruitment, and travel for terrorist purposes. Establishes specialized anti-terrorism courts and the Financial Intelligence Unit (CTAF). Criticized by human rights groups for overly broad definitions and extended pre-charge detention.
Key provisions
  • Broad definition of "terrorist act" including threats to public order
  • Criminalization of glorification of terrorism and incitement
  • Criminalizes financing of terrorism and related financial flows
  • Prohibition on travel to conflict zones for terrorist purposes
  • Specialized Terrorism Chamber at the Court of First Instance of Tunis
  • Extended detention periods for terrorism suspects
  • Asset freezing without prior judicial authorization
SecurityCriminal

Law No. 2019-73 of December 31, 2019Internal Security Forces Statute

Enacted: December 31, 2019 | In force: 2020
Governs the organization, status, and conduct of Tunisia's internal security forces (National Police and National Guard). Defines missions, hierarchy, disciplinary procedures, and accountability mechanisms. Establishes an independent oversight body for police conduct complaints.
SecurityAdministrative

State of Emergency Decree — Decree No. 78-50 of January 26, 1978

Enacted: January 26, 1978 | In force: 1978 | Continuously renewed since November 2015
Establishes the legal framework for declaring and managing states of emergency. Grants expanded executive powers including restriction of movement, house arrest, administrative detention, and closure of meeting places. Tunisia declared a state of emergency in November 2015 (after the Bardo Museum and Sousse beach attacks) and has renewed it without interruption since — now its tenth consecutive year. The most recent renewal, Décret n° 2026-15 of January 30, 2026 (JORT), extended the nationwide state of emergency declared by Décret n° 2025-485 of December 29, 2025 through December 31, 2026.
SecurityConstitutional

Military Justice Code — Law No. 86-79 of September 9, 1986 (amended 2019)

Enacted: September 9, 1986 | In force: 1986 | Amended: 2019
Governs military offenses and the organization of military courts (tribunaux militaires). Military courts have jurisdiction over offenses by military personnel and certain offenses against national defense. The 2019 amendment restricted jurisdiction of military courts over civilians — prohibiting trial of civilians before military courts except for offenses directly related to the armed forces — a significant human rights improvement.
SecurityCriminal

Weapons Law — Law No. 69-33 of June 12, 1969

Enacted: June 12, 1969 | Published: JORT N°23, June 13, 1969 | Implementing decree: Decree No. 70-60 of February 21, 1970
Regulates the import, trade, possession, and carrying of weapons and ammunition in Tunisia. Classifies weapons into five categories: military weapons, defensive/sporting weapons, hunting weapons, historical/collectible weapons, and air rifles. No unified "Code des Armes" exists — the framework remains this single 1969 law plus implementing decrees, with no confirmed later amendment identified.
SecurityCriminal

🛂 Nationality, Immigration & Asylum

Nationality Code — Law No. 63-6 of February 28, 1963

Enacted: February 28, 1963 | In force: 1963 | Amended: 1975, 2002, 2010
Defines Tunisian nationality by birth, descent, naturalization, and reacquisition. Originally limited transmission of nationality through the paternal line; the 2010 amendment allows Tunisian mothers married to foreign nationals to transmit nationality to their children. Governs loss, renunciation, and deprivation of nationality. Naturalization requires 5 years of lawful residence. Pending reform: a bill filed at the ARP on February 27, 2026 by 36 co-sponsoring deputies (now identifiable as projet de loi n° 17/2026) would tighten naturalization by requiring proof of legal/regular entry into Tunisia plus 7 consecutive years of legal residence (up from 5), while easing rules for stateless persons born in Tunisia and creating an expedited "exceptional interest" naturalization track for high-skill foreign nationals. The ARP Rights and Freedoms Commission examined the bill at a session held May 7, 2026, which press coverage of that session also described as addressing a requirement that a stateless child's parents hold legal Tunisian residency status for the child's citizenship eligibility. Under committee review as of this update; not yet adopted.
Key provisions
  • Art. 6: Nationality by birth to a Tunisian father (jus sanguinis)
  • Art. 12: Naturalization requires 5-year lawful residence
  • 2010 amendment: children of Tunisian mother and foreign father may acquire nationality
  • Art. 30–35: Loss and deprivation of Tunisian nationality
NationalityCivil

Law No. 68-7 of March 8, 1968Entry and Residence of Foreigners

Enacted: March 8, 1968 | In force: 1968 | Amended: 1975, 2004
Governs the entry, stay, and expulsion of foreign nationals in Tunisia. Requires visa for most non-Maghreb nationals (bilateral visa exemptions apply). Establishes residence permit categories and procedures. Governs administrative detention pending deportation. Tunisia has no comprehensive asylum law; refugee status was determined by UNHCR under its mandate, but in June 2024 Tunisian authorities instructed UNHCR to suspend new registration, pre-registration, and refugee-status-determination processing (services to already-registered refugees continued); the government's stated rationale was an intent to establish a national asylum system, which had not been enacted as of Human Rights Watch's 2025 and 2026 World Report chapters on Tunisia.
NationalityAdministrative

EU–Tunisia Memorandum of UnderstandingStrategic and Global Partnership

Signed: July 16, 2023
Non-binding political declaration between the EU and Tunisia covering five pillars: macroeconomic stability, trade and investment, green energy, people-to-people contacts, and — most emphasized in implementation — migration and mobility. The EU pledged roughly €105 million for border-control capacity, €150 million in direct budget support, and a conditional €900 million in macro-financial assistance tied to an IMF program Tunisia ultimately declined to conclude. Tunisia committed to combating smuggling networks, conducting search-and-rescue operations in its territorial waters, and accepting returns of third-country nationals. The MOU bypassed parliamentary ratification on both sides. In January 2025 the European Commission overhauled its disbursement mechanism following reports of abuses by EU-funded security forces against migrants; Tunisian officials have since pushed to renegotiate the arrangement toward greater reciprocity. Update (Run 43): marking the MOU's third anniversary, a joint letter dated July 1, 2026 from Human Rights Watch, Amnesty International, the International Commission of Jurists, the Committee to Protect Journalists, EuroMed Rights, and Avocats Sans Frontières was sent to European Commission President Ursula von der Leyen calling for a full human-rights review of the partnership and conditionality on further EU funding, citing continued reports of abuses against migrants in Tunisian territory. Forms the policy backdrop to the domestic 2023–2026 migrant-policy shift above and the EU's 2026 "safe country of origin" designation below.
NationalityAdministrative

EU Designation of Tunisia as a "Safe Country of Origin"EU Pact on Migration and Asylum

European Parliament vote: February 10, 2026 (408–184–60) | Council approval: February 23, 2026 | Rules in force: June 12, 2026
Not Tunisian domestic law, but an EU-law development directly affecting Tunisian nationals abroad: under the EU's Pact on Migration and Asylum, the European Parliament and Council designated Tunisia one of several countries presumed "safe" EU-wide, subjecting Tunisian asylum-seekers in the EU to accelerated, presumptive-rejection border-procedure examination of their claims from June 12, 2026 onward. Distinct from, but connected to, the domestic 2023–2026 migrant-policy shift and UNHCR-registration suspension documented above and the 2023 EU-Tunisia migration MOU above. Flagged as a single-source-class finding from this run pending independent corroboration of the exact vote tallies and in-force date against a European Parliament or Official Journal of the EU primary text.
NationalityUnconfirmed

Irregular Migration Law — Law No. 2004-6 of February 3, 2004

Enacted: February 3, 2004 | In force: 2004
Criminalizes irregular emigration by Tunisian nationals ("harraga") with penalties of up to 3 years imprisonment. Criticized by human rights organizations as criminalizing movement. Used to prosecute individuals attempting to cross to Europe irregularly. Tunisia also receives migrants from sub-Saharan Africa; their status is governed by the 1968 Immigration Law and UNHCR procedure. Note: No domestic asylum law as of 2026; draft asylum law discussed since 2013 but not enacted.
NationalityCriminal

National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad — Law No. 2016-68 of August 3, 2016 (reform bill pending, 2024–2026)

Enacted: August 3, 2016 | In force: 2016 | Implementing decree: Government Decree No. 2019-820 of September 10, 2019 | Reform bill: n° 2024-064, under ARP committee review since 2024
Creates the National Council for Tunisians Residing Abroad (Conseil National des Tunisiens Résidant à l'Étranger), a consultative public body with legal personality and administrative/financial autonomy, seated in Tunis and attached to the ministry responsible for Tunisians abroad. Represents Tunisia's diaspora (estimated at over 1.5 million people) before public authorities and issues an annual activity report to the President, the ARP Speaker, and the Head of Government. Government Decree No. 2019-820 (September 10, 2019) set the Council's administrative and financial organization. A reform bill (projet de loi n° 2024/064) has been under continued examination by the ARP's Foreign Affairs, International Cooperation, Tunisians Abroad, and Migration Committee since 2024, with a working session held June 4, 2026 on its text, followed by a further joint hearing on June 22, 2026 with the President of the National Council for Tunisians Abroad herself. The bill would restructure the Council's general assembly, introduce individual, direct, voluntary-basis voting (including electronic voting) for diaspora representatives on a gender-parity basis — replacing the current framework's limited effective functioning since 2016 — and reposition the Council as a horizontal coordination body unifying public policy across ministries dealing with Tunisians abroad and mobilizing expatriate expertise for national economic development. Not yet adopted as of this run.
NationalityAdministrativePending

⚖️ Judicial Organization

Judicial Organization Law — Law No. 2013-13 of April 2, 2013 (as amended 2022)

Enacted: April 2, 2013 | In force: 2013 | Amended: 2022
Organizes the court system of Tunisia: Cantonal Courts (minor matters), Courts of First Instance (24, one per governorate), Courts of Appeal (3 — Tunis, Sousse, Sfax), Court of Cassation (apex civil/criminal court, Tunis), Administrative Court (apex administrative jurisdiction), and the Constitutional Court (established under 2022 Constitution). Specialized jurisdictions include the Terrorism Chamber at TFI Tunis.
Court structure
  • Cantonal Courts: minor civil matters, misdemeanor infractions
  • Courts of First Instance (24): civil, commercial, criminal chambers
  • Courts of Appeal (3 — Tunis, Sousse, Sfax): appellate jurisdiction
  • Court of Cassation (Tunis): reviews points of law only
  • Administrative Court: all public law disputes
  • Terrorism Chamber: specialized jurisdiction at TFI Tunis level
  • Constitutional Court: established under 2022 Constitution (Arts. 116–120)
AdministrativeConstitutional

Judicial Statute — Law No. 67-29 of July 14, 1967 (as amended)

Enacted: July 14, 1967 | In force: 1967 | Amended: 1997, 2013, 2022
Governs the appointment, promotion, transfer, discipline, and removal of judges. Establishes the Superior Council of the Judiciary (Conseil Supérieur de la Magistrature — CSM) as the body responsible for judicial governance. Post-2022, the CSM's composition was changed by presidential decree, significantly reducing judicial independence according to the UN Special Rapporteur on the Independence of Judges and Lawyers. Update (Run 40): tensions over judicial and legal-profession independence escalated in mid-2026. The Tunisian National Bar Association (ONAT) staged an escalating protest campaign — a May 13 Bar Council escalation vote, a symbolic May 18 red-armband action, then rallies at courts in Tunis (Bab Bnet), Bizerte, Béja, Jendouba, Le Kef, and Siliana — culminating in a nationwide lawyers' general strike on June 18, 2026 demanding reform of the profession's legal framework and stronger judicial-independence guarantees; Bâtonnier Boubaker Belthabet called it a success, but no government response had followed as of late June 2026, and on June 9, 2026 forty international legal and human-rights organizations issued a joint statement condemning an escalating campaign against lawyers, judges, and the Bar. Separately, judge Anas Hmaidi — president of the Association of Tunisian Magistrates (AMT), re-elected at its February 2026 congress despite government pressure — was sentenced to one year in prison on April 6, 2026, prompting condemnation from the International Union of Magistrates and the African Commission on Human and Peoples' Rights, both linking the prosecution to the continued absence of a permanent, independent Superior Judicial Council (see the Provisional Supreme Judicial Council entry below). Update (Run 42): the Tunis Court of Appeal's correctional chamber confirmed Hmaidi's one-year sentence on July 1, 2026 in a ruling issued by default (par défaut/in absentia), this time ordering immediate execution of the sentence — a harsher procedural posture than the first-instance judgment. TAP/Diwan FM reporting confirmed the same legal qualification (Art. 136 Penal Code, obstruction of the freedom to work) tied to his conduct at a June 13, 2022 hearing in Monastir; Hmaidi continues to dispute the legitimacy of the Provisional Supreme Council of the Judiciary body that lifted his immunity on September 20, 2022. Update (Run 43): ONAT's annual general assembly was reported scheduled for July 11, 2026, a potential venue for the Bar to set conditions for renewed dialogue with the government following the unresolved June 18, 2026 general strike; no government response to the strike's demands had been reported as of this run. Update (Run 46): confirmed and refined — the July 11, 2026 assembly is not an elective session (Belthabet was already elected bâtonnier for a 2025–2028 term in September 2025); Belthabet has framed its purpose as convening the Bar Council and general assembly to "evaluate the evolution of the dispute [with the authorities] and adopt consequent measures," i.e., a strategy review on whether to escalate or seek renewed dialogue. Separately, the public prosecutor at the Tunis Court of Appeal filed an appeal challenging the quorum/procedural validity of an earlier lawyers' extraordinary general assembly, with a hearing reported set for June 10, 2026 — outcome not independently confirmed. As of this run (July 2, 2026), the government still had not proposed any official or informal mediation, per Belthabet, though ONAT says it remains open to dialogue. Update (Run 60): on July 2, 2026, UN Special Rapporteur on the independence of judges and lawyers Margaret Satterthwaite publicly stated that Hmaidi's conviction "should have been overturned," a statement the AMT amplified in its own denunciation of the July 1 appeals ruling as unfair — the first UN Special Procedures reaction specifically naming this case, distinct from the broader CSM-composition criticism already cited above.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Decree-Law No. 2022-11 of February 12, 2022Provisional Supreme Judicial Council

Enacted: February 12, 2022 | Published: JORT No. 2022-016
The specific decree-law referenced above: dissolved the elected Supreme Judicial Council (created under Organic Law No. 2016-34) and replaced it with a "Provisional Supreme Council of the Judiciary," composed predominantly of senior ex officio magistrates plus members appointed by the President from candidates submitted via the Prime Ministry. Retains three specialized sub-councils (judicial, administrative, financial). Remains the operative — still "provisional" — body as of mid-2026, with no permanent council established to replace it.
AdministrativeConstitutional

Decree-Law No. 2022-35 of June 1, 2022Magistrate Dismissal Power (Completing Decree-Law No. 2022-11)

Enacted: June 1, 2022
Grants the President of the Republic power to summarily dismiss any magistrate by simple presidential decree in cases of declared "emergency," threat to public security, or harm to "the country's superior interest," based on a report from unspecified "competent authorities." Dismissal triggers automatic criminal referral; the dismissed magistrate cannot appeal until after a final criminal judgment. Used the same day to dismiss 57 judges and prosecutors, condemned by HRW, Amnesty International, and EuroMed Rights; the Tunisian Association of Magistrates continued to denounce the dismissals as unresolved injustice as of June 2026.
AdministrativeConstitutionalCriminal

Organic Law No. 2013-53 of December 24, 2013Transitional Justice

Enacted: December 24, 2013 | In force: 2014
Establishes Tunisia's transitional justice framework including the Truth and Dignity Commission (IVD — Instance Vérité et Dignité). Covers: truth-seeking, reparations, institutional reform, and accountability for human rights violations committed between July 1, 1955 and December 31, 2013. IVD operated from 2014–2019; issued final report in 2019 documenting systematic torture, political persecution, and economic crimes.
AdministrativeConstitutionalCriminal

"State Security Conspiracy" Prosecutions and Related 2025–2026 Convictions

Arrests from: February 2023 | Case 1 sentences: April 19, 2025 | Case 1 appeal: November 27–28, 2025 | Case 2 appeal: February 2, 2026 | Bensedrine sentenced: June 26, 2026
A mass prosecution of dozens of opposition politicians, lawyers, and activists on charges of conspiracy against state security, prosecuted under general Penal Code state-security provisions (Arts. 72–85, see Criminal Law section) rather than a dedicated statute — added here as a standalone entry given its significance to Tunisia's post-2021 rule-of-law trajectory, previously undocumented in this artifact despite 39 prior research runs. Case 1 (defendants including Khayam Turki, Ridha Belhaj, Ghazi Chaouachi, Jaouhar Ben Mbarek, Issam Chebbi, and Abdelhamid Jelassi) produced severe sentences on April 19, 2025, described by Human Rights Watch as a "sham trial"; on appeal (Nov 27–28, 2025), 34 of 37 sentences (5–45 years) were upheld and 3 defendants acquitted (Lazher Akremi, Hattab Ben Slama, Noureddine Boutar), with several sentences increased (Chebbi, Ben Mbarek, Chaouachi, and Belhaj raised from 18 to 20 years) while businessman Kamel Ltaief's sentence was reduced from 66 to 45 years, remaining the heaviest in the case. A related but separate Case 2, focused on Ennahda party leadership, produced an appeals ruling on February 2, 2026 sentencing roughly 20 defendants to 3–35 years; Ennahda leader Rached Ghannouchi's sentence was raised from 14 to 20 years (56 years cumulative across his cases), and co-defendants Kamel Guizani, Rafik Abdessalem, Lotfi Zitoun, and Mouadh Ghannouchi received 35 years in absentia — Amnesty International described the ruling as deepening a "rule-of-law crisis." Separately, Sihem Bensedrine — journalist and former president of the Truth and Dignity Commission (IVD, see the Transitional Justice entry above) — was sentenced to 25 years in prison on June 26, 2026, among the most severe individual sentences of a rights defender since 2021. Opposition politician Abir Moussi (Free Destourian Party leader, detained since October 2023) also had a Tunis Court of Appeal ruling reported around March 2026, condemned by Amnesty International as unjust. See the Judicial Statute entry above for the related prosecution of AMT president judge Anas Hmaidi, whose one-year sentence was confirmed on appeal (with immediate execution ordered) on July 1, 2026 — Run 42 update. Update (Run 60): a related but distinct terrorism-chamber case opened July 3, 2026: the Tunis Court of First Instance's terrorism chamber rejected pre-trial release requests (June 30, 2026) for Ennahda secretary-general Ajmi Lourimi and co-defendant Mossaâb Gharbi, prosecuted under the Anti-Terrorism Law (Law No. 2015-26, see Criminal Law section) for failing to report a terrorism-related offense; no verdict has been reported as of this run.
CriminalConstitutional

Mediation Law — Law No. 2002-82 of August 3, 2002

Enacted: August 3, 2002 | In force: 2002
Establishes the legal framework for civil and commercial mediation as an alternative dispute resolution (ADR) mechanism. Mediators must be registered and trained. A mediation agreement, once signed by parties and approved by the competent court, is enforceable as a court judgment. The Startup Act (2018) encourages mediation for commercial disputes involving labeled startups. Reduces court caseloads and offers faster, confidential dispute resolution.
JudicialCivil

Notaries Law — Law No. 94-60 of May 23, 1994(Run 44 correction)

Enacted: May 23, 1994 | Predecessor: Law No. 88-27 of July 12, 1988
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited this statute as "Law No. 67-39 of June 8, 1967" — no such law could be found in any source searched (NATLEX, jurisitetunisie.com, legislation-securite.tn), and it appears to have been a fabricated citation. The verified currently-operative statute, confirmed directly from jurisitetunisie.com's full-text reproduction and cited in the preamble of the 2011 decree-law reorganizing the lawyer profession, is Law No. 94-60 of May 23, 1994 — 65 articles across 8 chapters (registration on the professional roll; powers; legal status; rights/obligations; discipline; regional chambers and national association; transitional provisions). It separated the notarial function from that of court bailiff (huissier de justice), a split not present in the probable predecessor, Law No. 88-27 of July 12, 1988. Notaries are public officers authenticating deeds, contracts, inheritance declarations, and real property transactions; notarial acts are enforceable without court order, and all real property transfers, mortgage deeds, and company-formation documents must be notarized. Supervised by regional chambers of notaries and a national professional association.
JudicialCivil

Law No. 87-79 of December 29, 1987 (as amended)Lawyers Statute (Barreau de Tunisie)

Enacted: December 29, 1987 | In force: 1988 | Amended: 1999, 2009
Governs the legal profession and bar association in Tunisia. The Bar Association (Ordre National des Avocats de Tunisie — ONAT) is the self-regulatory body for all lawyers admitted to the Tunisian bar. Sets qualification requirements (law degree + 3-year pupillage), bar admission conditions, and continuing education obligations. Regulates fee structures, professional conduct, confidentiality (avocat-client privilege), conflict of interest rules, and disciplinary procedures. Guarantees lawyers' immunity from prosecution for oral or written statements made in the exercise of their functions before courts.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–10: Bar membership; qualification requirements; pupillage (stage) period
  • Art. 11–20: Professional rights — audience before all courts; freedom of speech in court
  • Art. 21–28: Inviolability of lawyer-client communications; prohibition of office searches without ONAT involvement
  • Art. 29–36: Professional conduct rules; prohibition of conflicts of interest
  • Art. 37–44: Fee arrangements; prohibition of contingency fees as primary arrangement
  • Art. 45–52: ONAT governance — National Council, regional bars (Tunis, Sousse, Sfax, etc.)
  • Art. 53–60: Disciplinary council; sanctions ranging from warning to permanent disbarment
JudicialAdministrative

Law No. 2002-52 of June 3, 2002Legal Aid (Aide Judiciaire) (Run 44 correction)

Enacted: June 3, 2002 | Amended: Law No. 2007-27 of May 7, 2007
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited this statute as "Law No. 2002-42 of April 3, 2002" — no such law could be verified in any source searched, and it appears to have been a fabricated citation. The verified statute, confirmed via legislation-securite.tn and jurisitetunisie.com's "bureau d'aide judiciaire" page, is Law No. 2002-52 of June 3, 2002, amended by Law No. 2007-27 of May 7, 2007 (extending eligibility to criminal cassation appeals). Establishes Tunisia's legal aid system for civil and criminal matters: any natural person lacking sufficient income to cover court/enforcement costs without compromising basic necessities may apply, at any stage of proceedings; non-profit legal persons and foreigners (subject to reciprocity) also qualify. Applications are decided by a Legal Aid Bureau attached to each Court of First Instance, chaired by the Republic Prosecutor with a Finance Ministry representative and a lawyer as members; decisions are not subject to appeal, though a new application may be resubmitted. A separate, older track — Decree No. 74-882 of September 26, 1974, replaced by Organic Law No. 2011-2 of January 3, 2011 — governs legal aid specifically before the Administrative Court.
JudicialSocial

Law No. 66-49 of July 2, 1966 (as amended)Judicial Officers (Huissiers de Justice)

Enacted: July 2, 1966 | In force: 1966 | Amended: 1995, 2002, 2011
Regulates the profession of judicial officers (huissiers de justice) in Tunisia — officers of the court responsible for serving legal process, executing judgments, and conducting judicial auctions (ventes aux enchères). Huissiers are public officers appointed by the Ministry of Justice; they hold a monopoly on service of process and enforcement of court judgments. Regulated by the National Chamber of Huissiers (Chambre Nationale des Huissiers de Justice). The 2011 amendment introduced electronic service of process as a supplementary method. Pending reform: a modernization bill (digitized archives, revised access conditions to the profession, expanded legal protections for huissiers) is under review, supported by the professional order; a provision within it would additionally allow mutual-consent divorce to be finalized before a huissier-notaire without court involvement, which women's rights groups have criticized as weakening Personal Status Code judicial safeguards for wives. Not yet enacted.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–8: Status of huissier as public officer; appointment by Ministry of Justice
  • Art. 9–16: Territorial jurisdiction; offices by judicial circuit
  • Art. 17–24: Service of process procedures (summons, notifications, judgments)
  • Art. 25–32: Enforcement of civil judgments (seizure of assets, evictions, expulsions)
  • Art. 33–38: Conduct of judicial auctions of seized movable property
  • Art. 39–44: Fee schedule (tariff set by ministerial decree)
  • Art. 45–50: Professional discipline; liability for service errors
JudicialCivilPending

Extradition & Mutual Legal Assistance — Code of Criminal Procedure, Book IV, Ch. VIII (Arts. 308–335)

Base code: Law No. 68-23 of July 24, 1968 | Extradition chapter amended by Law No. 93-113 of November 22, 1993
Tunisia has no standalone extradition or mutual-legal-assistance statute — the current cybercrime entry elsewhere in this artifact notes only in passing that the Budapest Convention "complement[s]" extradition mechanisms, without a dedicated entry for the underlying domestic framework. Extradition of foreigners is instead governed entirely by Book IV, Chapter VIII of the Code of Criminal Procedure (conditions of extradition, procedure, and effects), supplemented by bilateral and multilateral treaties — e.g., the 1972 France–Tunisia judicial-cooperation and extradition convention. Tunisian nationals cannot be extradited; political offenses and torture risk are recognized grounds for refusal.
JudicialCriminal

🌍 International Law & Key Treaties

Key International Conventions Ratified by Tunisia

Various dates
Tunisia is party to numerous international conventions forming part of domestic law upon ratification (monist approach per Art. 20 of the 2022 Constitution).
Major conventions
  • UN Charter (1956) — upon independence
  • ICCPR — ratified March 18, 1969
  • ICESCR — ratified March 18, 1969
  • CEDAW — ratified September 20, 1985 (reservations partially lifted 2011)
  • Convention on the Rights of the Child — ratified January 29, 1992
  • UN Convention Against Torture — ratified September 23, 1988
  • African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights — ratified 1983
  • Arab Charter on Human Rights — ratified 2008
  • New York Convention on Arbitral Awards (1958) — ratified July 22, 1967
  • ILO Core Conventions — 62 conventions ratified
  • TRIPS Agreement — WTO member since March 29, 1995
  • WIPO Copyright Treaty — ratified 2012
  • Patent Cooperation Treaty (PCT) — member since March 1, 2001
  • UNCAC — ratified October 23, 2008
  • Paris Agreement on Climate Change — ratified July 3, 2017
  • UNCLOS — ratified April 24, 1985
  • EU-Tunisia Association Agreement — in force March 1, 1998
Constitutional

Withdrawal of the Article 34(6) DeclarationProtocol to the African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights on the Establishment of an African Court

Reported: March 2026
Tunisia reportedly withdrew its declaration under Article 34(6) of the Ouagadougou Protocol establishing the African Court on Human and Peoples' Rights, which had permitted individuals and NGOs to file complaints directly with the Court without first exhausting proceedings before the African Commission. If confirmed, the withdrawal would close that direct-access route for Tunisian complainants going forward, mirroring earlier withdrawals by Rwanda, Tanzania, Benin, and Côte d'Ivoire, and would mark a rollback of one of Tunisia's post-2011 international human-rights-law commitments (Tunisia ratified the underlying African Charter in 1983 and the Protocol establishing the Court separately). Flagged as a single-source finding from this run's research pass, pending stronger corroboration (e.g., an African Court press release or JORT/Ministry of Foreign Affairs text) in a future run — consistent with this artifact's practice of noting unverified leads rather than silently omitting or overstating them.
ConstitutionalUnconfirmed

EU-Tunisia Association Agreement — Law No. 97-37 of July 1, 1997

Signed: July 17, 1995 | Ratified by Tunisia: July 1, 1997 | In force: March 1, 1998
Association agreement between Tunisia and the European Union — the first Euro-Mediterranean Partnership agreement. Established a free trade area for manufactured goods, progressively eliminating tariffs by 2008. Includes provisions on services, capital, intellectual property, and political dialogue. Supplemented by Action Plans under the European Neighbourhood Policy. DCFTA (Deep and Comprehensive Free Trade Area) negotiations launched 2016, not yet concluded as of 2026.
CommercialConstitutional

Arab Maghreb Union Treaty — 1989 (Marrakesh Treaty)

Signed: February 17, 1989 | Ratified: 1989
Treaty establishing the Arab Maghreb Union (UMA) among Tunisia, Algeria, Morocco, Libya, and Mauritania. Aims for eventual economic integration and free movement. In practice largely dormant due to Algeria-Morocco tensions. Bilateral agreements within the AMU framework remain in force, including free movement protocols for Maghreb nationals within the region.
CommercialConstitutional

Decree No. 23 of 2025Ratification of the North Western Sahara Aquifer System Consultation Mechanism

Underlying agreement signed: April 24, 2024 (Algeria, tripartite agriculture-ministers meeting) | Ratifying decree published: JORT Issue No. 4, January 11, 2025
A two-article decree: Art. 1 ratifies the tripartite agreement establishing a consultation mechanism ("mécanisme de concertation") for shared management of the North Western Sahara Aquifer System (SASS/NWSAS), a transboundary fossil groundwater resource shared by Tunisia, Algeria, and Libya and critical to southern Tunisia's water supply; Art. 2 covers publication. Algeria ratified the same agreement in parallel.
EnvironmentalConstitutional

UNCLOS Ratification — April 24, 1985

Ratified: April 24, 1985 | Convention in force: November 16, 1994
Tunisia ratified the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS). Defines maritime zones: 12-nautical-mile territorial sea, 24 nm contiguous zone, and 200 nm Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ). Bilateral maritime delimitation agreements concluded with Italy (1971, continental shelf) and Libya (boundary established by ICJ ruling in 1982). Tunisia's EEZ in the Mediterranean is significant for fisheries and hydrocarbon exploration rights.
ConstitutionalTransport

WTO Membership and Bilateral Trade Agreements

WTO membership: March 29, 1995 | Ongoing bilateral and regional agreements
Tunisia is a WTO member and party to multiple bilateral and regional trade agreements: Pan-Arab Free Trade Area (PAFTA, 1997); EU Association Agreement (1998); Agadir Agreement with Egypt, Jordan, and Morocco (2007, implementing diagonal cumulation of origin); EFTA-Tunisia Free Trade Agreement (2005). DCFTA talks with the EU and negotiations for African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA) integration remain active as of 2026.
CommercialConstitutional

Agadir Agreement — Law No. 2004-20 of February 24, 2004 (ratification) | Operational: 2007

Agreement signed: February 25, 2004 | Ratified by Tunisia: February 24, 2004 | Operational: March 2007
The Agadir Agreement on the establishment of a free trade zone between Tunisia, Morocco, Egypt, and Jordan — four countries with EU Association Agreements. Eliminates tariffs and non-tariff barriers on trade in goods between member states. A key feature is the application of diagonal cumulation of origin with the EU, allowing products manufactured from inputs originating in any member country to benefit from EU preferential tariffs. Managed by the Agadir Technical Unit (ATU) based in Amman. Tunisia's exports to Egypt and Jordan are primarily industrial goods, olive oil, and chemicals. The agreement fits within the Euro-Mediterranean Partnership framework and enhances supply chain integration between North Africa and the Middle East.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–5: Establishment of the free trade zone; objectives
  • Art. 6–10: Tariff elimination schedule for industrial goods (immediate for most)
  • Art. 11–15: Rules of origin: Pan-Euro-Mediterranean cumulation rules apply
  • Art. 16–20: Agricultural goods: partial liberalization with tariff-rate quotas for sensitive items
  • Art. 21–25: Trade facilitation: simplified customs procedures, single document
  • Art. 26–30: Dispute resolution mechanism; role of ATU
  • Art. 31–35: Safeguard clauses; anti-dumping provisions
CommercialConstitutional

AfCFTA Ratification — Law No. 2021-19 of June 7, 2021

Agreement adopted: March 21, 2018 | Tunisia ratified: June 7, 2021 | Trading began: January 1, 2021 (for earlier ratifiers)
Tunisia ratified the Agreement Establishing the African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA) in June 2021, becoming one of 44 countries to ratify by that date (out of 55 AU member states). The AfCFTA creates the world's largest free trade area by number of participating countries, covering 1.3 billion people and a GDP of approximately $3.4 trillion. Phase I covers trade in goods (tariff reduction) and trade in services. Phase II covers investment, intellectual property, competition, and e-commerce. Tunisia hopes to use the AfCFTA to diversify its export markets beyond Europe (currently ~70% of trade), targeting sub-Saharan Africa's consumer markets, particularly for processed foods, pharmaceuticals, textiles, and ICT services.
Key provisions
  • Tariff reduction: 90% of tariff lines phased to zero over 5–10 years (LDC treatment: 15 years)
  • Sensitive goods: 7% of tariff lines; longer liberalization schedules
  • Excluded goods: 3% (limited exclusions for security or development reasons)
  • Rules of origin: negotiated per sector; generally 25–35% local value addition
  • AfCFTA Secretariat in Accra, Ghana manages dispute resolution and implementation
  • Services schedules: Phase II negotiations ongoing; Tunisia targeting professional services and ICT
CommercialConstitutional

🚢 Transport Law

Maritime Commercial Code — Law No. 62-13 of April 24, 1962(as amended) (Run 44 correction)

Enacted: April 24, 1962 | In force: July 1, 1962 | Amended: 1972, 1990, 1998, 2004-3
Run 44 correction: prior runs cited "Law No. 62-36 of March 27, 1962" — no such law could be verified, and it appears to have been a fabricated citation. NATLEX (ILO's legislative database) directly confirms the actual founding instrument as Law No. 62-13 of April 24, 1962, "portant promulgation du Code de commerce maritime," entering into force July 1, 1962; corroborated by droit-afrique.com/FAOLEX and the Ministry of Transport's own consolidated text. Comprehensive statute governing merchant shipping, maritime contracts, and maritime liability: registration/flagging of vessels, maritime liens and mortgages (ranked above ordinary privileges), carriage of goods incorporating the 1924 Brussels Convention (Hague Rules) — carrier liability capped at 400 dinars per package since a 1990 decree (up from 100). Law No. 2004-3 (January 20, 2004) replaced the ship-ownership/registration articles. Maritime ports managed by OMMP (Office de la Marine Marchande et des Ports).
TransportCommercial

Merchant Marine Organization Law — Law No. 95-33 of April 14, 1995

Enacted: April 14, 1995 | In force: 1995
Governs professions in the Tunisian merchant marine: maritime officers, engineers, and seafarers. Establishes training, certification, and competency requirements for seafarers (implementing the STCW Convention — Standards of Training, Certification and Watchkeeping). Sets working conditions, wages, and social protection for crew members. Regulates the Tunisian ship register and conditions for flagging vessels. Enforced by the OMMP and the Merchant Navy General Secretariat (created by Decree 2019-144).
TransportLabor

Maritime Ports Code — Law No. 2009-48 of July 8, 2009

Enacted: July 8, 2009 | In force: 2009 | Replaced: Law No. 99-25 (commercial ports) and Law No. 2002-47 (fishing ports)
Unifies the legal regime for Tunisia's commercial and fishing ports into a single code, repealing the previously separate 1999 commercial-ports law and 2002 fishing-ports law. Governs port administration, concessions, safety, and access; administered by OMMP (Office de la Marine Marchande et des Ports), the same authority referenced under the Maritime Commercial Code above, of which this ports-specific code had not previously had its own standalone entry.
TransportAdministrative

Civil Aviation Code — Law No. 99-58 of June 29, 1999

Enacted: June 29, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Implementing law: 98-110 (OACA)
Governs civil aviation in Tunisia: aircraft registration, airworthiness certification, pilot licensing, air navigation rules, airport operations, and air carrier liability. The Office of Civil Aviation and Airports (OACA — Office de l'Aviation Civile et des Aéroports), created by Law No. 98-110 of December 28, 1998, manages 7 international airports (Tunis-Carthage, Djerba-Zarzis, Sfax-Thyna, Monastir-Habib Bourguiba, Tozeur-Nefta, Tabarka, Gafsa-Ksar). Tunisia is an ICAO member. National carrier: Tunisair (state-owned). Domestic aviation partially opened to private carriers.
Key provisions
  • Aircraft registration: Tunisian nationality mark TS-
  • OACA: national aviation authority; safety, security, airspace management
  • Air carrier liability: aligned with Montreal Convention (ratified)
  • Airport concessions: governed by OACA and Ministry of Transport
  • Private carrier licensing: open skies principles for international routes (EU Horizontal Agreement 2021)
TransportCommercial

Road Traffic Code — Law No. 99-71 of July 26, 1999 (as amended) (Run 50 correction)

Enacted: July 26, 1999 | Adopted by Chamber of Deputies: July 6, 1999 | Published: JORT No. 61, July 30, 1999 | In force: 6 months after publication | Amended: 2001-101, 2003, 2014, 2019
Run 50 correction: prior runs cited an enactment date of "August 23, 1999" — this could not be verified, and two independent sources (jurisitetunisie.com's promulgation-law page and a matching targeted web search) instead confirm July 26, 1999 as the date of Law No. 99-71, adopted by the Chamber of Deputies on July 6, 1999 and published in JORT No. 61 of July 30, 1999; treat July 26 as the correct date absent a primary JORT text showing otherwise. Governs road traffic, vehicle registration, driver licensing, traffic offenses, and penalties in Tunisia. Sets speed limits (50 km/h urban, 90 km/h interurban, 110 km/h highways), blood alcohol limits (0.3‰ — stricter than EU), seat belt and helmet requirements, and mobile phone restrictions. Amended by Law No. 2001-101 of October 22, 2001, among later amendments; the 2014 amendment introduced a points-based driving license system and increased penalties for serious traffic offenses, and the 2019 amendment strengthened sanctions for drunk driving and speeding. Road safety authority under the Ministry of Transport. Pending reform: a "violence routière" (road violence) reform package — automatic driving-license withdrawal for speeding 10 km/h or more over the limit, expanded radar/camera enforcement, and a zero-tolerance regime — was approved as a draft bill by the Council of Ministers on July 28, 2025 (not, as sometimes reported, an already-enacted law of that date) and forwarded to the ARP; as of the most recent confirmed reporting (December 2025) it remained under ARP examination, with no JORT promulgation number identified as of June 2026.
Transport

Land Transport Law — Law No. 2004-33 of April 19, 2004

Enacted: April 19, 2004 | In force: 2004
Regulates land transport of passengers and goods in Tunisia. Governs the licensing of transport operators (buses, taxis, trucks, coaches). Establishes the Land Transport Observatory. Sets vehicle technical inspection requirements and roadworthiness standards. Regulates freight transport contracts (lettre de voiture). Governs special transport (hazardous goods, heavy loads, exceptional convoys). National carrier TRANSTU operates urban buses and metro in Tunis; regional bus networks operated by SRTG companies.
TransportCommercial

⚡ Energy & Natural Resources

Hydrocarbons Code — Law No. 99-93 of August 17, 1999 (amended 2002, 2004, 2008, 2017)

Enacted: August 17, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Last amended: Law 2017-41 of May 30, 2017
Primary legal framework for oil and gas exploration and production in Tunisia. Governs concession agreements, production sharing contracts, and service contracts. State oil company ETAP (Entreprise Tunisienne d'Activités Pétrolières) holds mandatory minimum participation. The 2017 amendment required parliamentary approval of new hydrocarbon contracts and introduced transparency obligations aligned with EITI standards. Tunisia has been an EITI member since 2016.
Key provisions
  • Prospecting permit: maximum 1 year
  • Exploration permit: maximum 5 years (with 2 extensions of 2 years each)
  • Production concession: maximum 30 years (renewable)
  • ETAP mandatory participation: minimum share in all concessions
  • CIT rate: reduced to 50% if ETAP holds ≥40% stake (vs. standard 75% otherwise)
  • Art. 59: Mandatory compliance with environmental, health and safety standards
  • 2017 amendment: parliamentary approval required for contracts; EITI alignment
EnergyCommercial

Mining Code — Law No. 2003-30 of April 28, 2003 (as amended)

Enacted: April 28, 2003 | In force: 2003 | Amended: 2010
Governs exploration and exploitation of non-hydrocarbon mineral resources (phosphates, iron ore, lead, zinc, fluorite, salt, sand, limestone). Key provisions: 3-year exploration licenses; 30-year mining concessions; 1% royalty on production value payable semi-annually; 25% CIT on mining profits with a 5-year tax holiday from production start. Enforced by the National Office of Mines (ONM — Office National des Mines). Tunisia is the world's 5th largest phosphate producer; Compagnie des Phosphates de Gafsa (CPG) is the principal public mining entity. Pending reform: the Ministry of Industry called in July 2025 for accelerated finalization of a mining code revision framed around reinforcing state sovereignty over natural resources; as of mid-2026 no revised code had been adopted.
Key provisions
  • Exploration license: 3 years, renewable
  • Mining concession: 30 years, renewable
  • Royalty: 1% of production value (semi-annual payments)
  • Tax holiday: 5 years from production commencement
  • CIT rate: 25% on mining profits
  • Environmental rehabilitation bond: mandatory for concession holders
  • Artisanal mining rights: recognized in designated areas
EnergyCommercial

Electricity and Gas Law — Law No. 62-8 of April 3, 1962 (and Law No. 2002-28 of March 25, 2002)

STEG created: April 3, 1962 | Market opening: Law 2002-28
Law 62-8 created the Société Tunisienne de l'Electricité et du Gaz (STEG) as the national monopoly utility for electricity generation, transmission, distribution, and natural gas distribution. STEG remains a state monopoly for the transmission grid and distribution network. Law No. 2002-28 (March 25, 2002) partially opened electricity generation to private Independent Power Producers (IPPs) under power purchase agreements (PPAs) with STEG, while maintaining STEG's grid monopoly. Supervised by the Ministry of Industry and Energy.
EnergyCommercial

Renewable Energy Law — Law No. 2015-12 of May 11, 2015 (amended by Law 2019-47)

Enacted: May 11, 2015 | Amended: 2019 (business climate law)
Establishes three authorization regimes for electricity production from renewable sources: (1) concession regime for large projects (>30 MW); (2) authorization regime for medium projects (1–30 MW) selling to STEG under feed-in tariff PPA; (3) self-consumption regime for own-use generation. National target: 35% of electricity from renewables by 2030. The 2019 Investment Climate Law expanded self-consumption to allow inter-site wheeling by companies. ANME (National Agency for Energy Conservation) coordinates renewable energy development. Recent application: the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts (CNRD) approved, on May 13, 2026, five concession-regime agreements (TND ~1.6 billion combined) for utility-scale photovoltaic plants in Sidi Bouzid, Gafsa, and Gabès governorates — the El Khabna plant (198 MW) plus four 100 MW plants — with all output to be injected exclusively into the STEG grid under the law's concession-regime PPA framework.
EnergyEnvironmental

📊 Public Finance & Budget

Organic Budget Law — Law No. 2004-42 of May 13, 2004

Enacted: May 13, 2004 | In force: 2004 | Replaced: Law No. 67-53 of 1967
Defines the constitutional and legal rules for preparation, adoption, execution, and control of Tunisia's state budget. Organizes the annual Finance Law (loi de finances) process: Cabinet submits draft to parliament by October 15; parliament must adopt by December 31. Establishes budget program classification (Title I: personnel costs; Title II: investment). Governs supplementary budgets and carryover authorizations. Budget reform toward performance-based budgeting has been discussed but not fully implemented.
Key provisions
  • Finance Law submitted to parliament by October 15 each year
  • Parliamentary vote required by December 31; if not, prior-year budget extended by decree
  • Title I: recurrent expenditures (staff, goods, services, debt service)
  • Title II: development expenditures (public investment)
  • Special accounts (comptes spéciaux du Trésor) regulated separately
  • Court of Accounts certifies final state accounts (loi de règlement)
AdministrativeFinancial

Court of Accounts — Law No. 68-8 of March 8, 1968 (as amended, most recently Law 2019-56)

Enacted: March 8, 1968 | In force: 1968 | Major reform: Law 2019-56
Tunisia's supreme audit institution (Cour des Comptes / ديوان المحاسبات). Audits central government, public enterprises, and local authority accounts. Issues binding rulings (arrêts) imposing financial liability (débet) on public accountants for unauthorized expenditures. Issues annual public report on state budget execution presented to parliament. Audit opinions required before adoption of final budget accounts (loi de règlement). The 2019 reform modernized audit methods and strengthened transparency of findings. Also plays a role in asset declaration review.
AdministrativeFinancial

Public Debt Management — Law No. 97-42 of July 28, 1997 and annual Finance Laws

Framework law: July 28, 1997 | Debt limits: set annually in Finance Law
Governs the state's borrowing authority and public debt management. The General Treasury of Tunisia manages the debt portfolio. External borrowing requires parliamentary authorization in the annual Finance Law. Domestic instruments: Treasury bills (BTC — 13/26/52 weeks), medium-term Treasury bonds (OAT). Tunisia has issued Eurobonds and Sukuk (Islamic bonds) on international capital markets (2012, 2015, 2020, 2021). IMF extended credit facility (2016–2020, revised 2023). Public debt reached approximately 80% of GDP by 2024.
FinancialAdministrative

Presidential Decree No. 2026-108Afreximbank US$500 Million Financing Facility

BCT–Afreximbank facility signed: June 16, 2026 | Presidential decree published: JORT, June 8, 2026 | BCT board approval: March 18, 2026
Under the Public Debt Management framework (Law No. 97-42) and the external-financing authorization in the Finance Law 2026, the Central Bank of Tunisia (BCT), acting on the state's behalf, concluded a US$500 million (≈TND 1.471 billion) financing facility with the African Export-Import Bank (Afreximbank) to help cover the FY2026 budget's external financing needs, including strategic imports of energy and food products. Signed by President Kais Saied as Presidential Decree No. 2026-108, ratifying the BCT board's March 2026 decision. Part of a broader financing relationship under which Afreximbank has extended roughly TND 5.15 billion to Tunisia over four years.
FinancialAdministrative

Draft Laws n° 38/2026 & n° 39/2026 (pending)State Guarantees for STEG IBRD Loans (~US$430 Million)

Underlying loan agreements signed: November 3, 2025 | Finance & Budget Committee approval: June 28, 2026 | Status: awaiting plenary vote as of July 2, 2026
Two companion government bills authorizing state guarantees for International Bank for Reconstruction and Development (IBRD/World Bank) loans to the Société Tunisienne de l'Électricité et du Gaz (STEG). Draft Law n° 38/2026 guarantees a US$400 million loan extended directly to STEG; Draft Law n° 39/2026 guarantees a complementary US$30 million loan channeled through the IBRD in its capacity as implementing entity of the Clean Technology Fund (CTF). Both finance a STEG energy-sector modernization program aimed at improving the utility's operational efficiency, financial performance, and governance, against a backdrop of STEG's reported TND 7.356 billion total indebtedness and over TND 6 billion in unpaid receivables (per a June 27, 2026 La Presse report). The ARP Finance and Budget Committee approved both texts on June 28, 2026; they were transmitted to the ARP plenary for final adoption but had not been put to a plenary vote as of this update. Distinct from the unrelated "combating financial exclusion" bill (Draft Law n° 023/2024 — see entry below), which some secondary sources conflated with these same bill numbers; the 38/2026 and 39/2026 numbering is independently confirmed for the STEG guarantees via the ARP's official bill registry and Arabic-language press (لجنة المالية، مشروع قانون عدد 38 و39 لسنة 2026).
FinancialEnergyPending

2026–2030 National Development Plan (Bill)

Filed under Constitution Arts. 75 & 77 | ARP standing-committee examination: June 29–30, 2026 | Status: pending plenary vote as of July 1, 2026
The economic and social planning framework law for 2026–2030, formally deposited at the ARP as draft law n°42/2026 on June 17, 2026 following its adoption by the Council of Ministers. Structured as a single-article bill — the Assembly votes yes/no/abstain on the plan as a whole rather than amending individual provisions, per committee rapporteur Saber Jlassi — it was examined jointly by all Assembly of People's Representatives standing committees on June 29–30, 2026 but not yet put to a plenary vote. Committee discussion noted the prior five-year plan's execution rate stood at under 40%, a benchmark cited by critical deputies alongside the new plan's more ambitious targets. Targets raising the average annual growth rate to 4.2% over the period (versus 2.4% in the prior five-year period), reaching 5.1% by 2030, and raising investment from roughly 15.5% to 20% of GDP via a public-investment project portfolio of approximately TND 102 billion. A June 30, 2026 joint hearing with Minister of Economy and Planning Samir Abdelhafidh preceded committee deliberations, followed by a second joint ARP audition of the Minister on July 1, 2026 to gather further committee input (Tunisian media noted the plan's limited treatment of the media/audiovisual sector as a specific point raised in that session, and a July 1, 2026 report noted several deputies voicing doubts about the feasibility of the plan's growth and investment targets, with the Finance Committee separately flagging concerns over the underlying economic and financial assumptions). Separately on July 1, 2026, the National Union of Tunisian Journalists (SNJT) publicly criticized the plan by name for omitting a dedicated media-sector strategy, calling for the assembly to formally recognize media as an economic and cultural sector, incorporate press-freedom legislation, and include the sector in the plan's monitoring mechanisms. The plan's headline targets include lowering the poverty rate to under 15%, a portfolio of roughly 21,100 public-investment projects funded at approximately TND 102 billion, and raising the renewable-energy share of electricity production to 35% by 2030; committee coverage also noted that of 40,748 development proposals submitted by local councils, 34,715 were retained in the plan's regional components. The standing committees are compiling a consolidated synthesis report for submission to the ARP Bureau, which will then schedule the plenary session before the text proceeds to the Conseil National des Régions et des Districts under Art. 77. Once adopted, it is expected to serve as the legal reference framework underpinning the national AI strategy and the NDC 3.0 climate financing plan already noted elsewhere in this document. Not yet enacted — the most active legislative process in Tunisia as of this update.
FinancialConstitutionalPending

🌾 Agriculture & Fisheries

Fisheries Code — Law No. 94-13 of January 31, 1994 (as amended 2002, 2014)

Enacted: January 31, 1994 | In force: 1994 | Amended: 2002, 2014
Comprehensive statute governing fishing activities in Tunisian territorial waters and the EEZ. Commercial fishing vessels require licenses from the Ministry of Agriculture. Sets fishing quotas, prohibited fishing methods (dynamite, poison, trawling within 3 nm of coast), protected marine species, and seasonal closures. Establishes the National Fisheries Office (ONP — Office National des Pêches) for market organization and port management. Tuna and swordfish fishing regulated under ICCAT commitments. Aquaculture (fish farming) licensing governed by supplementary 2006 decree.
AgricultureEnvironmental

Plant Health Law — Law No. 99-24 of February 23, 1999; Veterinary Law — Law No. 2005-35 of May 25, 2005

Plant Health: February 23, 1999 | Veterinary: May 25, 2005
The Plant Health Law governs protection of crops from pests and diseases, phytosanitary border inspections, and pesticide registration and use (administered by INAP — National Institute for Plant Protection). The Veterinary Law regulates animal health surveillance, veterinary practice licensing, slaughterhouse standards, and control of notifiable animal diseases. Both laws implement Tunisia's WTO Agreement on Sanitary and Phytosanitary Measures (SPS Agreement) obligations for agricultural trade.
AgricultureHealth

Agricultural Development Law — Law No. 71-72 of December 31, 1971 and Law No. 2000-30 of March 20, 2000

Development Areas: 1971 | Irrigated Perimeters: March 20, 2000
The 1971 law empowers the Ministry of Agriculture to designate agricultural development areas with state support for irrigation and land improvement. The 2000 law governs management of public irrigated perimeters (périmètres irrigués publics — PIPs): water pricing through GDA user associations (Groupements de Développement Agricole), infrastructure maintenance, and cost recovery. Emergency water restrictions (2023 drought) were issued under the Water Code's provisions with inter-ministerial coordination.
AgricultureLand

Organic Agriculture Law — Law No. 99-30 of April 5, 1999

Enacted: April 5, 1999 | In force: 1999 | Amended: 2009
Establishes the legal framework for organic farming, certification, labeling, and inspection in Tunisia. Organic products must be certified by an accredited certification body and labeled with the national organic mark. Controls use of synthetic pesticides, fertilizers, and GMOs on certified organic land. Administered by the Ministry of Agriculture and the Office for Organic Farming Development (BIO). Tunisia exports significant volumes of certified organic olive oil and dates to EU markets.
AgricultureCommercial

Law No. 67-41 of June 24, 1967 (as amended)Agricultural Cooperatives

Enacted: June 24, 1967 | In force: 1967 | Amended: 1982, 2001, 2020
Establishes the legal framework for agricultural cooperatives (coopératives agricoles) in Tunisia. Agricultural cooperatives are governed by a hybrid regime combining principles from general cooperative law and specific agricultural provisions. Types include: production cooperatives, service cooperatives (input supply, marketing), and credit cooperatives (Caisses de Crédit Agricole Mutuels). Registration with the Ministry of Agriculture and the Office of Agricultural Credit Cooperatives (ODCO) is required. Cooperatives benefit from tax exemptions on profits and reduced registration fees. The Tunisian Cooperative Union (UTAP — Union Tunisienne de l'Agriculture et de la Pêche) represents cooperatives at national level. The 2020 amendment strengthened governance requirements and introduced digital voting for cooperative assemblies.
Key provisions
  • Art. 1–10: Definition of agricultural cooperative; democratic governance (one member, one vote)
  • Art. 11–20: Types: production, marketing, supply, credit cooperatives
  • Art. 21–28: Registration requirements; statutes; minimum capital
